<span style="font-size: 120%;">↓</span> <a href="/wiki/Future" title="Future">Future</a> <span class="nowrap">  </span></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="background:#f8eaba;padding:0.4em;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_history" title="Template:Human history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_history" title="Template talk:Human history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>history of Africa</b> begins with the <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">emergence</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human" title="Archaic human">archaic humans</a> and - around 300–250,000 years ago—<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i>), in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>, and continues unbroken into the present as a patchwork of diverse and politically developing <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> The earliest known <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a> <a href="/wiki/Classical_African_civilization" title="Classical African civilization">arose</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>, and later in <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, North African history became entwined with the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a> while the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> swept from modern day <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> (Central Africa) across much of the sub-Saharan continent in waves between around 1000 BC and 1 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/AD" title="AD">AD</a>, creating a linguistic commonality across much of the central and Southern continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> spread west from Arabia to Egypt, crossing the Maghreb and the Sahel. Some notable pre-colonial states and societies in Africa include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ajuran_Empire" title="Ajuran Empire">Ajuran Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Kitara" title="Empire of Kitara">Bachwezi Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%27mt" title="D'mt">D'mt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal Sultanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a>, Warsangali Sultanate, <a href="/wiki/Buganda" title="Buganda">Buganda Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Nri" title="Kingdom of Nri">Kingdom of Nri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bono_state" title="Bono state">Bono State</a>, <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benin_Empire" title="Benin Empire">Benin Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo Empire</a>, Kingdom of Lunda (Punu-yaka), <a href="/wiki/Ashanti_Empire" title="Ashanti Empire">Ashanti Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mossi_Kingdoms" title="Mossi Kingdoms">Mossi Kingdoms</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutapa_Empire" title="Mutapa Empire">Mutapa Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mapungubwe" title="Kingdom of Mapungubwe">Kingdom of Mapungubwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sine" title="Kingdom of Sine">Kingdom of Sine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sennar_(sultanate)" title="Sennar (sultanate)">Kingdom of Sennar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Saloum" title="Kingdom of Saloum">Kingdom of Saloum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Baol" title="Kingdom of Baol">Kingdom of Baol</a>, Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Cayor" title="Cayor">Cayor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Zimbabwe" title="Kingdom of Zimbabwe">Kingdom of Zimbabwe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</a>, Empire of <a href="/wiki/Kaabu" title="Kaabu">Kaabu</a>, Kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ile_Ife" title="Ile Ife">Ile Ife</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Ancient Carthage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" title="Aksumite Empire">Aksumite Empire</a>. At its peak, prior to European <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>, it is estimated that Africa had up to 10,000 different states and autonomous groups with distinct languages and customs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the late 15th century, Europeans joined the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-5">[5]</a></sup> That includes the triangular trade, with the Portuguese initially acquiring slaves through trade and later by force as part of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a>. They transported enslaved <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern</a> Africans overseas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Subsequently, European colonization of Africa developed rapidly from around 10% (1870) to over 90% (1914) in the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a> (1881–1914). However following struggles for independence in many parts of the continent, as well as a weakened Europe after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_World_War" title="Second World War">Second World War</a> <span class="nowrap">(1939–1945)</span>, <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonization</a> took place across the continent, culminating in the 1960 <a href="/wiki/Year_of_Africa" title="Year of Africa">Year of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Disciplines such as recording of <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">oral history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">historical linguistics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Africa" title="Archaeology of Africa">archaeology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> have been vital in rediscovering the great African civilizations of antiquity.
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886046785">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-5 .toclevel-4 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-6 .toclevel-5 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-7 .toclevel-6 ul{display:none}</style><div class="toclimit-3"><div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-73"><a href="#20th_century"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">20th century</span></a>
<ul>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-74"><a href="#World_War_I"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">World War I</span></a></li>

<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-76"><a href="#French_Africa"><span class="tocnumber">5.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">French Africa</span></a></li>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-77"><a href="#World_War_II:_Military"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">World War II: Military</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-78"><a href="#Post-war_Africa:_decolonization"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Post-war Africa: decolonization</span></a>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_North_Africa" title="Prehistoric North Africa">Prehistoric North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa#Prehistory" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa § Prehistory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_West_Africa" title="Prehistoric West Africa">Prehistoric West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa#Prehistory" title="Central Africa">Central Africa § Prehistory</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa#Prehistory" title="East Africa">East Africa § Prehistory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa#Prehistory" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa § Prehistory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/African_archaeology" title="African archaeology">African archaeology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Paleolithic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">Lower Paleolithic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Stone_Age" title="Middle Stone Age">Middle Stone Age</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Later_Stone_Age" title="Later Stone Age">Later Stone Age</a></div>
<p>The first known <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">hominids</a> evolved in Africa. According to <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, the early hominids' skull anatomy was similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorilla</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_apes" title="Great apes">great apes</a> that also evolved in Africa, but the hominids had adopted a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biped" title="Biped">bipedal</a> locomotion which freed their hands. This gave them a crucial advantage, enabling them to live in both forested areas and on the open <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> at a time when Africa was drying up and the savanna was encroaching on forested areas. This would have occurred 10 to 5 million years ago, but these claims are controversial because biologists and genetics have humans appearing around the last 70 thousand to 200 thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 4 million years ago, several <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">australopithecine</a> hominid species had developed throughout <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">Eastern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>. They were tool users, and makers of tools. They scavenged for meat and were omnivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>By approximately 3.3 million years ago, primitive stone tools were first used to scavenge kills made by other predators and to harvest carrion and marrow from their bones. In hunting, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i> was probably not capable of competing with large predators and was still more prey than hunter. <i>H. habilis</i> probably did steal eggs from nests and may have been able to catch small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">game</a> and weakened larger prey (cubs and older animals). The tools were classed as <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._2–3_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._2–3-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1.8 million years ago, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i> first appeared in the fossil record in Africa. From <i>Homo ergaster</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> evolved 1.5 million years ago. Some of the earlier representatives of this species were still fairly small-brained and used primitive stone tools, much like <i>H. habilis</i>. The brain later grew in size, and <i>H. erectus</i> eventually developed a more complex stone tool technology called the <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a>. Possibly the first hunters, <i>H. erectus</i> mastered the art of making fire and was the first hominid to leave Africa, colonizing most of <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a> and perhaps later giving rise to <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></i>. Although some recent writers have suggested that <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_georgicus" title="Homo georgicus">Homo georgicus</a></i> was the first and primary hominid ever to live outside Africa, many scientists consider <i>H. georgicus</i> to be an early and primitive member of the <i>H. erectus</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="585" decoding="async" height="332" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg/140px-Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg/210px-Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg/280px-Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg 2x" width="140"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pieza_foli%C3%A1cea_africana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>African biface artifact (spear point) dated in Late Stone Age period</div></div></div>
<p>The fossil record shows <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> (also known as "modern humans" or "anatomically modern humans") living in Africa by about 350,000-260,000 years ago. The earliest known <i>Homo sapiens</i> fossils include the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud" title="Jebel Irhoud">Jebel Irhoud</a> remains from Morocco (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 315,000 years ago</span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20170607a_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20170607a-12">[12]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Florisbad_Skull" title="Florisbad Skull">Florisbad Skull</a> from South Africa (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 259,000 years ago</span>), and the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a> from Ethiopia (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 233,000 years ago</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HublinBen-Ncer2017_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-HublinBen-Ncer2017-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vidal22_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vidal22-17">[17]</a></sup> Scientists have suggested that <i>Homo sapiens</i> may have arisen between 350,000 and 260,000 years ago through a merging of populations in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20190910_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20190910-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20190910_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20190910-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence of a variety of behaviors indicative of <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">Behavioral modernity</a> date to the African <a href="/wiki/Middle_Stone_Age" title="Middle Stone Age">Middle Stone Age</a>, associated with early <i>Homo sapiens</i> and their emergence. Abstract imagery, widened subsistence strategies, and other "modern" behaviors have been discovered from that period in Africa, especially South, North, and East Africa. The <a href="/wiki/Blombos_Cave" title="Blombos Cave">Blombos Cave</a> site in South Africa, for example, is famous for rectangular slabs of <a href="/wiki/Ochre" title="Ochre">ochre</a> engraved with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geometric" title="Geometric">geometric</a> designs. Using multiple dating techniques, the site was confirmed to be around 77,000 and 100–75,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henshilwood_2002_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henshilwood_2002-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henshilwood_et_al._2009_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henshilwood_et_al._2009-21">[21]</a></sup> Ostrich egg shell containers engraved with geometric designs dating to 60,000 years ago were found at <a href="/wiki/Diepkloof_Rock_Shelter" title="Diepkloof Rock Shelter">Diepkloof</a>, South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Texier_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Texier-22">[22]</a></sup> Beads and other personal ornamentation have been found from Morocco which might be as much as 130,000 years old; as well, the Cave of Hearths in South Africa has yielded a number of beads dating from significantly prior to 50,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McBrearty_Brooks_2000_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-McBrearty_Brooks_2000-23">[23]</a></sup> and shell beads dating to about 75,000 years ago have been found at Blombos Cave, South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henshilwood_et_al._2004_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henshilwood_et_al._2004-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d'Errico_et_al._2005_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-d'Errico_et_al._2005-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanhaeren_et_al._2013_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanhaeren_et_al._2013-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specialized projectile weapons as well have been found at various sites in Middle Stone Age Africa, including bone and stone arrowheads at South African sites such as <a href="/wiki/Sibudu_Cave" title="Sibudu Cave">Sibudu Cave</a> (along with an early bone needle also found at Sibudu) dating approximately 60,000-70,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backwell_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backwell-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lombard_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lombard-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lombard_M_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lombard_M-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backwell2018_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backwell2018-31">[31]</a></sup> and bone harpoons at the Central African site of Katanda dating to about 90,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Evidence also exists for the systematic heat treating of silcrete stone to increase its flake-ability for the purpose of toolmaking, beginning approximately 164,000 years ago at the South African site of <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle_Point" title="Pinnacle Point">Pinnacle Point</a> and becoming common there for the creation of microlithic tools at about 72,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Early stone-tipped projectile weapons (a characteristic tool of <i>Homo sapiens</i>), the stone tips of javelins or throwing spears, were discovered in 2013 at the Ethiopian site of <a href="/wiki/Gademotta" title="Gademotta">Gademotta</a>, and date to around 279,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SahlePLOS1_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SahlePLOS1-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, an ochre processing workshop likely for the production of paints was uncovered dating to ca. 100,000 years ago at Blombos Cave, South Africa. Analysis shows that a liquefied pigment-rich mixture was produced and stored in the two abalone shells, and that ochre, bone, charcoal, grindstones and hammer-stones also formed a composite part of the toolkits. Evidence for the complexity of the task includes procuring and combining raw materials from various sources (implying they had a mental template of the process they would follow), possibly using pyrotechnology to facilitate fat extraction from bone, using a probable recipe to produce the compound, and the use of shell containers for mixing and storage for later use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.com-15257259_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.com-15257259-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Washington_Post-2011/10/12/gIQApyHrhL_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Washington_Post-2011/10/12/gIQApyHrhL-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Henshilwood_et_al._2011_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Henshilwood_et_al._2011-38">[38]</a></sup>
Modern behaviors, such as the making of shell beads, bone tools and arrows, and the use of ochre pigment, are evident at a Kenyan site by 78,000-67,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Expanding subsistence strategies beyond big-game hunting and the consequential diversity in tool types has been noted as signs of behavioral modernity. A number of South African sites have shown an early reliance on aquatic resources from fish to shellfish. <a href="/wiki/Pinnacle_Point" title="Pinnacle Point">Pinnacle Point</a>, in particular, shows exploitation of marine resources as early as 120,000 years ago, perhaps in response to more arid conditions inland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marean_et_al_2007_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marean_et_al_2007-40">[40]</a></sup> Establishing a reliance on predictable shellfish deposits, for example, could reduce mobility and facilitate complex social systems and symbolic behavior. Blombos Cave and Site 440 in Sudan both show evidence of fishing as well. Taphonomic change in fish skeletons from Blombos Cave have been interpreted as capture of live fish, clearly an intentional human behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McBrearty_Brooks_2000_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-McBrearty_Brooks_2000-23">[23]</a></sup> Humans in North Africa (<a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_North_Africa" title="Prehistoric North Africa">Nazlet Sabaha</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>) are known to have dabbled in <a href="/wiki/Chert" title="Chert">chert</a> <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mining</a>, as early as ≈100,000 years ago, for the construction of <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tools</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Records2015_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Records2015-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence was found in 2018, dating to about 320,000 years ago, at the Kenyan site of <a href="/wiki/Olorgesailie" title="Olorgesailie">Olorgesailie</a>, of the early emergence of modern behaviors including: long-distance trade networks (involving goods such as obsidian), the use of pigments, and the possible making of projectile points. It is observed by the authors of three 2018 studies on the site, that the evidence of these behaviors is approximately contemporary to the earliest known <i>Homo sapiens</i> fossil remains from Africa (such as at Jebel Irhoud and Florisbad), and they suggest that complex and modern behaviors began in Africa around the time of the emergence of <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR-593591796_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-593591796-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Atlantic-555674_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Atlantic-555674-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooks_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brooks-46">[46]</a></sup> In 2019, further evidence of early complex projectile weapons in Africa was found at <a class="external text" href="https://discoverafricanart.com/tribes/aduma-people/Adouma" rel="nofollow">Adouma</a>, Ethiopia dated 80,000-100,000 years ago, in the form of points considered likely to belong to darts delivered by spear throwers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahle_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahle-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 65–50,000 years ago, the species' expansion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Single-origin_hypothesis" title="Single-origin hypothesis">out of Africa</a> launched the colonization of the planet by modern human beings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Posth_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Posth-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vai_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vai-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-51">[51]</a></sup> By 10,000 BC, <i>Homo sapiens</i> had spread to most corners of <a href="/wiki/Afro-Eurasia" title="Afro-Eurasia">Afro-Eurasia</a>. Their dispersals are traced by linguistic, cultural and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._2–3_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._2–3-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The earliest physical evidence of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical activity</a> may be a <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a> found on the <a href="/wiki/Ishango_bone" title="Ishango bone">Ishango bone</a> dated to between 23,000 and 18,000 BC from in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> However, this interpretation of the object's purpose is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have argued that warfare was absent throughout much of humanity's prehistoric past, and that it emerged from more complex political systems as a result of <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentism</a>, agricultural <a href="/wiki/Farm" title="Farm">farming</a>, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, the findings at the site of <a href="/wiki/Nataruk" title="Nataruk">Nataruk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkana_County" title="Turkana County">Turkana County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, where the remains of 27 individuals who died as the result of an intentional attack by another group 10,000 years ago, suggest that inter-human conflict has a much longer history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_agriculture_and_desertification_of_the_Sahara">Emergence of agriculture and desertification of the Sahara</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emergence of agriculture and desertification of the Sahara">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution#In_Africa" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution § In Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3950" data-file-width="4156" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/290px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/435px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png/580px-Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Journal.pone.0076514.g004.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vegetation and water bodies in early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> (top), between about 12,000 and 7,000 years ago, and <a href="/wiki/Eemian" title="Eemian">Eemian</a> (bottom)</div></div></div>
<p>Around 16,000 BC, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Sea_Hills" title="Red Sea Hills">Red Sea Hills</a> to the northern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>, nuts, grasses and tubers were being collected for food. By 13,000 to 11,000 BC, people began collecting wild grains. This spread to <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a>, which domesticated its wild grains, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>. Between 10,000 and 8000 BC, <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Africa" title="Northeast Africa">Northeast Africa</a> was cultivating wheat and barley and raising sheep and cattle from Southwest Asia. A wet climatic phase in Africa turned the Ethiopian Highlands into a mountain forest. <a href="/wiki/Omotic_languages" title="Omotic languages">Omotic speakers</a> domesticated <a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">enset</a> around 6500–5500 BC. Around 7000 BC, the settlers of the Ethiopian highlands domesticated <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkeys</a>, and by 4000 BC domesticated donkeys had spread to Southwest Asia. <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic</a> speakers, partially turning away from cattle herding, domesticated <a href="/wiki/Teff" title="Teff">teff</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Finger_millet" title="Finger millet">finger millet</a> between 5500 and 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 11th millennium <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, pottery was independently invented in Africa, with the earliest pottery there dating to about 9,400 BC from central Mali.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-swissinfo_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-swissinfo-60">[60]</a></sup> It soon spread throughout the southern <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pottery_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pottery-61">[61]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannahs</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> in Northern West Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan speakers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mand%C3%A9_peoples" title="Mandé peoples">Mandé peoples</a> started to collect and domesticate wild millet, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_rice" title="African rice">African rice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> between 8000 and 6000 BC. Later, <a href="/wiki/Gourd" title="Gourd">gourds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Watermelon" title="Watermelon">watermelons</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castor_bean" title="Castor bean">castor beans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> were also collected and domesticated. The people started capturing wild cattle and holding them in circular thorn hedges, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> They also started making <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a> and built stone settlements (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tichitt" title="Tichitt">Tichitt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oualata" title="Oualata">Oualata</a>). <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">Fishing</a>, using bone-tipped <a href="/wiki/Harpoon" title="Harpoon">harpoons</a>, became a major activity in the numerous streams and lakes formed from the increased rains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Mande peoples have been credited with the independent development of agriculture about 4000–3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>In West Africa, the wet phase ushered in an expanding <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a> and wooded savanna from <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>. Between 9000 and 5000 BC, <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo speakers</a> domesticated the <a href="/wiki/Elaeis_guineensis" title="Elaeis guineensis">oil palm</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raffia_palm" title="Raffia palm">raffia palm</a>. Two seed plants, <a href="/wiki/Black-eyed_pea" title="Black-eyed pea">black-eyed peas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voandzeia" title="Voandzeia">voandzeia</a> (African groundnuts), were domesticated, followed by <a href="/wiki/Okra" title="Okra">okra</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kola_nut" title="Kola nut">kola nuts</a>. Since most of the plants grew in the forest, the Niger–Congo speakers invented polished stone axes for clearing forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> was occupied by <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_peoples" title="Pygmy peoples">pygmy peoples</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> who engaged in hunting and gathering. Some of the oldest <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> was produced by them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>For several hundred thousand years the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> has alternated between desert and savanna grassland in a 41,000 year cycle caused by changes ("<a href="/wiki/Precession" title="Precession">precession</a>") in the Earth's axis as it rotates around the sun which change the location of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_African_Monsoon" title="North African Monsoon">North African Monsoon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-67">[67]</a></sup> When the North African monsoon is at its strongest annual precipitation and subsequent vegetation in the Sahara region increase, resulting in conditions commonly referred to as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Sahara" title="Green Sahara">green Sahara</a>". For a relatively weak North African monsoon, the opposite is true, with decreased annual precipitation and less vegetation resulting in a phase of the Sahara climate cycle known as the "desert Sahara". The Sahara has been a desert for several thousand years, and is expected to become green again in about 15,000 years time (17,000 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakes_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakes-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Just prior to <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Saharan</a> desertification, the communities that developed south of Egypt, in what is now <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, were full participants in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neolithic_revolution" title="Neolithic revolution">Neolithic revolution</a> and lived a settled to semi-nomadic lifestyle, with domesticated plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> It has been suggested that <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megaliths</a> found at <a href="/wiki/Nabta_Playa" title="Nabta Playa">Nabta Playa</a> are examples of the world's first known <a href="/wiki/Archaeoastronomy" title="Archaeoastronomy">archaeoastronomical</a> devices, predating <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> by some 1,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The sociocultural complexity observed at Nabta Playa and expressed by different levels of authority within the society there has been suggested as forming the basis for the structure of both the Neolithic society at Nabta and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom of Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> By 5000 BC, Africa entered a dry phase, and the climate of the Sahara region gradually became drier. The population trekked out of the Sahara region in all directions, including towards the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Cataract" title="Second Cataract">Second Cataract</a>, where they made permanent or semipermanent settlements. A major climatic recession occurred, lessening the heavy and persistent rains in Central and Eastern Africa.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Africa">Central Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Central Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa#History" title="Central Africa">Central Africa § History</a></div>
<p>Archaeological findings in Central Africa have been discovered dating back to over 100,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Extensive walled sites and settlements have recently been found in Zilum, <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> approximately 60 km (37 mi) southwest of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> dating to the first millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade and improved agricultural techniques supported more sophisticated societies, leading to the early civilizations of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sao_civilization" title="Sao civilization">Sao</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shilluk_Kingdom" title="Shilluk Kingdom">Shilluk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Baguirmi" title="Kingdom of Baguirmi">Baguirmi</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wadai_Empire" title="Wadai Empire">Wadai</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppiahGates2010254_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppiahGates2010254-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around 1,000 BC, <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> migrants had reached the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Great Lakes Region</a> in Central Africa. Halfway through the first millennium BC, the Bantu had also settled as far south as what is now <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metallurgy">Metallurgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metallurgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Copper_metallurgy_in_Africa" title="Copper metallurgy in Africa">Copper metallurgy in Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron_metallurgy_in_Africa" title="Iron metallurgy in Africa">Iron metallurgy in Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_ornamental_staff_head,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/180px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/270px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/360px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_ornamental_staff_head,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>9th-century bronze staff head in form of a coiled snake, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Igbo-Ukwu" title="Archaeology of Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a>, Nigeria</div></div></div>
<p>Evidence of the early smelting of metals –  <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> –  dates from the fourth millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egyptians smelted copper during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predynastic_Egypt" title="Predynastic Egypt">predynastic period</a>, and bronze came into use after 3,000 BC at the latest<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>. Nubia became a major source of copper as well as of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The use of gold and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> in Egypt dates back to the predynastic period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehret_2002,_pp._136–137_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ehret_2002,_pp._136–137-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFr_Mountains" title="Aïr Mountains">Aïr Mountains</a> of present-day <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> people smelted copper independently of developments in the Nile valley between 3,000 and 2,500 BC. They used a process unique to the region, suggesting that the technology was not brought in from outside; it became more mature by about 1,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ehret_2002,_pp._136–137_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ehret_2002,_pp._136–137-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 1st millennium BC <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_working" title="Iron working">iron working</a> had reached <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Northwestern Africa</a>, Egypt, and Nubia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Zangato and Holl document evidence of iron-smelting in the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> that may date back to 3,000 to 2,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a> using iron weapons pushed Nubians out of Egypt in 670 BC, after which the use of iron became widespread in the Nile valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Falola_2002_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Falola_2002-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The theory that <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> spread to <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> via the Nubian city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meroe" title="Meroe">Meroe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> is no longer widely accepted, and some researchers<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2021)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe that sub-Saharan Africans invented iron metallurgy independently. <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">Metalworking</a> in West Africa has been dated as early as 2,500 BC at Egaro west of the <a href="/wiki/Termit_Massif" title="Termit Massif">Termit</a> in Niger, and iron working was practiced there by 1,500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Iron smelting has been dated to 2,000 BC in southeast <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eze-Uzomaka_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-Uzomaka-87">[87]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a> provides possible evidence of iron working as early as the 3rd millennium BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Iron smelting developed in the area between <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a> between 1,000 and 600 BC, and in West Africa around 2,000 BC, long before the technology reached Egypt. Before 500 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok culture</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Jos_Plateau" title="Jos Plateau">Jos Plateau</a> was already smelting iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuiver_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuiver-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (May 2021)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggert_2014_53–54_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggert_2014_53–54-94">[94]</a></sup> Archaeological sites containing iron-smelting furnaces and slag have been excavated at sites in the <a href="/wiki/Nsukka" title="Nsukka">Nsukka</a> region of southeast Nigeria in <a href="/wiki/Igboland" title="Igboland">Igboland</a>: dating to 2,000 BC at the site of <a href="/wiki/Lejja" title="Lejja">Lejja</a> (Eze-Uzomaka 2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eze-Uzomaka_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eze-Uzomaka-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl-2009_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-2009-95">[95]</a></sup> and to 750 BC and at the site of <a href="/wiki/Opi_(archaeological_site)" title="Opi (archaeological site)">Opi</a> (Holl 2009).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holl-2009_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holl-2009-95">[95]</a></sup> The site of Gbabiri (in the Central African Republic) has also yielded evidence of iron metallurgy, from a reduction furnace and blacksmith workshop; with earliest dates of 896-773 BC and 907-796 BC respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggert_2014_53–54_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eggert_2014_53–54-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The ancient <a href="/wiki/Classical_African_civilization" title="Classical African civilization">history</a> of North Africa is inextricably linked to that of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">Ancient Near East</a>. This is particularly true of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum</a> ruled modern-day <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the coastal area of the western part of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. The Ancient Egyptians established ties with the <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Land of Punt</a> in 2,350 BC. Punt was a trade partner of Ancient Egypt and it is believed that it was located in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> or <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> cities such as <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> were part of the Mediterranean <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> developed more or less independently in those times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Egypt">Ancient Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ancient Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Egypt" title="History of ancient Egypt">History of ancient Egypt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="691" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png/220px-Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png/330px-Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png/440px-Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lower_Egypt_Nomes_01.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Ancient Egypt and nomes</div></div></div>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>, settlement became concentrated in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a>, where numerous sacral chiefdoms appeared. The regions with the largest population pressure were in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Lower_Egypt" title="Lower Egypt">Lower Egypt</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a>, and also along the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">second and third cataracts</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Dongola_Reach" title="Dongola Reach">Dongola Reach</a> of the Nile in Nubia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> This population pressure and growth was brought about by the cultivation of southwest Asian crops, including wheat and barley, and the raising of sheep, goats, and cattle. Population growth led to competition for farm land and the need to regulate farming. Regulation was established by the formation of <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracies</a> among sacral chiefdoms. The first and most powerful of the chiefdoms was <a href="/wiki/Ta-Seti" title="Ta-Seti">Ta-Seti</a>, founded around 3,500 BC. The idea of sacral chiefdom spread throughout Upper and Lower Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2906" data-file-width="4372" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/220px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/330px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg/440px-All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:All_Gizah_Pyramids.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyramids_of_Giza" title="Pyramids of Giza">pyramids of Giza</a>, symbols of the civilization of ancient Egypt</div></div></div>
<p>Later consolidation of the chiefdoms into broader political entities began to occur in Upper and Lower Egypt, culminating into the unification of Egypt into one political entity by <a href="/wiki/Narmer" title="Narmer">Narmer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Menes" title="Menes">Menes</a>) in 3,100 BC. Instead of being viewed as a sacral chief, he became a <a href="/wiki/Imperial_cult" title="Imperial cult">divine king</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">henotheism</a>, or worship of a single god within a polytheistic system, practiced in the sacral chiefdoms along Upper and Lower Egypt, became the polytheistic <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Ancient Egyptian religion</a>. Bureaucracies became more centralized under the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaohs</a>, run by <a href="/wiki/Vizier_(Ancient_Egypt)" title="Vizier (Ancient Egypt)">viziers</a>, governors, tax collectors, generals, artists, and technicians. They engaged in tax collecting, organizing of labor for major public works, and building irrigation systems, <a href="/wiki/Pyramid" title="Pyramid">pyramids</a>, temples, and canals. During the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Fourth Dynasty of Egypt">Fourth Dynasty</a> (2,620–2,480 BC), long-distance trade was developed, with the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> for timber, with Nubia for gold and skins, with <a href="/wiki/Land_of_Punt" title="Land of Punt">Punt</a> for <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a>, and also with the western Libyan territories. For most of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a>, Egypt developed her fundamental systems, institutions and culture, always through the central bureaucracy and by the divinity of the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaoh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the fourth millennium BC, Egypt started to extend direct military and political control over her southern and western neighbors. By 2,200 BC, the Old Kingdom's stability was undermined by rivalry among the governors of the <a href="/wiki/Nome_(Egypt)" title="Nome (Egypt)">nomes</a> who challenged the power of pharaohs and by invasions of Asiatics into the Nile Delta. The <a href="/wiki/First_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="First Intermediate Period of Egypt">First Intermediate Period</a> had begun, a time of political division and uncertainty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Middle_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Middle Kingdom of Egypt">Middle Kingdom of Egypt</a> arose when Mentuhotep II of <a href="/wiki/Eleventh_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eleventh Dynasty of Egypt">Eleventh Dynasty</a> unified Egypt once again between 2041 and 2016 BC beginning with his conquering of <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Tenth Dynasty of Egypt">Tenth Dynasty</a> in 2041 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrimal1988155–156_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrimal1988155–156-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHabachi196316–52_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHabachi196316–52-101">[101]</a></sup> Pyramid building resumed, long-distance trade re-emerged, and the center of power moved from <a href="/wiki/Memphis,_Egypt" title="Memphis, Egypt">Memphis</a> to <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a>. Connections with the southern regions of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>, Wawat and Irthet at the second cataract were made stronger. Then came the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Second Intermediate Period of Egypt">Second Intermediate Period</a>, with the invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> on horse-drawn <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariots</a> and utilizing bronze weapons, a technology heretofore unseen in Egypt. Horse-drawn chariots soon spread to the west in the inhabitable Sahara and North Africa. The Hyksos failed to hold on to their Egyptian territories and were absorbed by Egyptian society. This eventually led to one of Egypt's most powerful phases, the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a> (1,580–1,080 BC), with the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth Dynasty of Egypt">Eighteenth Dynasty</a>. Egypt became a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a> controlling Nubia and <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> while exerting political influence on the Libyans to the West and on the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>As before, the New Kingdom ended with invasion from the west by Libyan princes, leading to the <a href="/wiki/Third_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Third Intermediate Period of Egypt">Third Intermediate Period</a>. Beginning with <a href="/wiki/Shoshenq_I" title="Shoshenq I">Shoshenq I</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-second_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-second Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-second Dynasty</a> was established. It ruled for two centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>To the south, Nubian independence and strength was being reasserted. This reassertion led to the conquest of Egypt by Nubia, begun by <a href="/wiki/Kashta" title="Kashta">Kashta</a> and completed by <a href="/wiki/Piye" title="Piye">Piye</a> (Pianhky, 751–730 BC) and <a href="/wiki/Shabaka" title="Shabaka">Shabaka</a> (716–695 BC). This was the birth of the <a href="/wiki/Twenty-fifth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-fifth Dynasty of Egypt</a>. The Nubians tried to re-establish Egyptian traditions and customs. They ruled Egypt for a hundred years. This was ended by an <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Assyrian</a> invasion, with <a href="/wiki/Taharqa" title="Taharqa">Taharqa</a> experiencing the full might of Assyrian iron weapons. The Nubian pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Tantamani" title="Tantamani">Tantamani</a> was the last of the Twenty-fifth dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>When the Assyrians and Nubians left, a new <a href="/wiki/Twenty-sixth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-sixth Dynasty of Egypt">Twenty-sixth Dynasty</a> emerged from <a href="/wiki/Sais,_Egypt" title="Sais, Egypt">Sais</a>. It lasted until 525 BC, when Egypt was invaded by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persians</a>. Unlike the Assyrians, the Persians stayed. In 332, Egypt was conquered by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>. This was the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemaic dynasty</a>, which ended with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egypt_(Roman_province)" title="Egypt (Roman province)">Roman conquest</a> in 30 BC. Pharaonic Egypt had come to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199130–33-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nubia">Nubia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nubia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nubia#History" title="Nubia">Nubia § History</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kerma_Culture" title="Kerma Culture">Kerma Culture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kingdom of Kush</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="486" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg/220px-Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg/330px-Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg/440px-Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kushite_empire_700bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Nubian Empire</a> at its greatest extent</div></div></div>

<p>Around 3,500 BC, one of the first sacral kingdoms to arise in the Nile was <a href="/wiki/Ta-Seti" title="Ta-Seti">Ta-Seti</a>, located in northern Nubia. Ta-Seti was a powerful sacral kingdom in the Nile Valley at the 1st and 2nd cataracts that exerted an influence over nearby chiefdoms based on pictorial representation ruling over <a href="/wiki/Upper_Egypt" title="Upper Egypt">Upper Egypt</a>. Ta-Seti traded as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syro-Palestine" title="Syro-Palestine">Syro-Palestine</a>, as well as with Egypt. Ta-Seti exported gold, copper, ostrich feathers, <a href="/wiki/Ebony" title="Ebony">ebony</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_trade" title="Ivory trade">ivory</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="Old Kingdom of Egypt">Old Kingdom</a>. By the 32nd century BC, Ta-Seti was in decline. After the unification of Egypt by <a href="/wiki/Narmer" title="Narmer">Narmer</a> in 3,100 BC, Ta-Seti was invaded by the Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Hor-Aha" title="Hor-Aha">Hor-Aha</a> of the <a href="/wiki/First_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="First Dynasty of Egypt">First Dynasty</a>, destroying the final remnants of the kingdom. Ta-Seti is affiliated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A-Group_Culture" title="A-Group Culture">A-Group Culture</a> known to archaeology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2020)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:History_of_Africa#Dubious" title="Talk:History of Africa">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="2788" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg/220px-Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg/330px-Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg/440px-Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Naqa_Apedamak_temple.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nubian Temple of <a href="/wiki/Apedemak" title="Apedemak">Apedemak</a>, Naqa</div></div></div>
<p>Small sacral kingdoms continued to dot the Nubian portion of the Nile for centuries after 3,000 BC. Around the latter part of the third millennium, there was further consolidation of the sacral kingdoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Two kingdoms in particular emerged: the Sai kingdom, immediately south of Egypt, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kerma" title="Kingdom of Kerma">Kingdom of Kerma</a> at the third cataract. Sometime around the 18th century BC, the Kingdom of Kerma conquered the Kingdom of Sai, becoming a serious rival to Egypt. Kerma occupied a territory from the first cataract to the confluence of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>, <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atbarah_River" title="Atbarah River">Atbarah River</a>. About 1,575 to 1,550 BC, during the latter part of the <a href="/wiki/Seventeenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Seventeenth Dynasty of Egypt">Seventeenth Dynasty</a>, the Kingdom of Kerma invaded Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The Kingdom of Kerma allied itself with the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a> invasion of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt eventually re-energized under the <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth Dynasty of Egypt">Eighteenth Dynasty</a> and conquered the Kingdom of Kerma or <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kush" title="Kingdom of Kush">Kush</a>, ruling it for almost 500 years. The Kushites were Egyptianized during this period. By 1100 BC, the Egyptians had withdrawn from Kush. The region regained independence and reasserted its culture. Kush built a new religion around <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> and made <a href="/wiki/Napata" title="Napata">Napata</a> its spiritual center. In 730 BC, the Kingdom of Kush invaded Egypt, taking over <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Egypt" title="Thebes, Egypt">Thebes</a> and beginning the Nubian Empire. The empire extended from <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> to the confluence of the Blue Nile, the White Nile, and River Atbara.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 664 BC, the Kushites were expelled from Egypt by iron-wielding <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrians</a>. Later, the administrative capital was moved from Napata to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mer%C3%B6e" title="Meröe">Meröe</a>, developing a new Nubian culture. Initially, Meroites were highly Egyptianized, but they subsequently began to take on distinctive features. Nubia became a center of iron-making and cotton cloth manufacturing. Egyptian writing was replaced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meroitic_alphabet" title="Meroitic alphabet">Meroitic alphabet</a>. The lion god <a href="/wiki/Apedemak" title="Apedemak">Apedemak</a> was added to the Egyptian pantheon of gods. Trade links to the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> increased, linking Nubia with Mediterranean <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greece</a>. Its architecture and art diversified, with pictures of lions, ostriches, giraffes, and elephants. Eventually, with the rise of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksum" title="Aksum">Aksum</a>, Nubia's trade links were broken and it suffered environmental degradation from the tree cutting required for iron production. In 350 AD, the Aksumite king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ezana" title="Ezana">Ezana</a> brought Meröe to an end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carthage">Carthage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Carthage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage#History" title="Ancient Carthage">Ancient Carthage § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginianempire.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2276" data-file-width="2424" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/220px-Carthaginianempire.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/330px-Carthaginianempire.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Carthaginianempire.PNG/440px-Carthaginianempire.PNG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carthaginianempire.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carthaginian Empire</div></div></div>
<p>The Egyptians referred to the people west of the Nile, ancestral to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berbers</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyans</a>. The Libyans were agriculturalists like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauri_(people)" title="Mauri (people)">Mauri</a> of <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Numidians" title="Numidians">Numidians</a> of central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>. They were also nomadic, having the horse, and occupied the arid pastures and desert, like the <a href="/wiki/Gaetuli" title="Gaetuli">Gaetuli</a>. Berber desert nomads were typically in conflict with Berber coastal agriculturalists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> were Mediterranean <a href="/wiki/Sailor" title="Sailor">seamen</a> in constant search for valuable metals such as copper, gold, tin, and lead. They began to populate the North African coast with settlements—trading and mixing with the native Berber population. In 814 BC, Phoenicians from <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a> established the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> By 600 BC, Carthage had become a major trading entity and power in the Mediterranean, largely through trade with tropical Africa. Carthage's prosperity fostered the growth of the Berber kingdoms, <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretania</a>. Around 500 BC, Carthage provided a strong impetus for trade with <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>. Berber middlemen, who had maintained contacts with Sub-Saharan Africa since the desert had desiccated, utilized pack animals to transfer products from oasis to oasis. Danger lurked from the <a href="/wiki/Garamantes" title="Garamantes">Garamantes</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fes" title="Fes">Fez</a>, who raided <a href="/wiki/Caravan_(travellers)" title="Caravan (travellers)">caravans</a>. Salt and metal goods were traded for gold, slaves, beads, and ivory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg/220px-Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg/330px-Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg/440px-Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ruines_de_Carthage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of Carthage</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Carthaginians</a> were rivals to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>. Carthage fought the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, three wars with Rome: the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a> (264 to 241 BC), over <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Second_Punic_War" title="Second Punic War">Second Punic War</a> (218 to 201 BC), in which <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> invaded Europe; and the <a href="/wiki/Third_Punic_War" title="Third Punic War">Third Punic War</a> (149 to 146 BC). Carthage lost the first two wars, and in the third it was destroyed, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Roman province of Africa</a>, with the Berber Kingdom of Numidia assisting Rome. The Roman province of Africa became a major agricultural supplier of wheat, <a href="/wiki/Olive" title="Olive">olives</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a> to <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial Rome</a> via exorbitant taxation. Two centuries later, Rome brought the Berber kingdoms of Numidia and Mauretania under its authority.
