In the 420's AD, <a href="/wiki/Vandal_Kingdom" title="Vandal Kingdom">Vandals</a> invaded North Africa and Rome lost her territories, subsequently the Berber kingdoms regained their independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> gained a foothold in Africa at <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in the 1st century AD and spread to <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Northwest Africa</a>. By 313 AD, with the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a>, all of Roman North Africa was Christian. <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> adopted <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysite</a> Christianity and formed the independent <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Coptic Church</a>. Berbers adopted <a href="/wiki/Donatism" title="Donatism">Donatist</a> Christianity. Both groups refused to accept the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_Berbers">Role of the Berbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Role of the Berbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2011</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As Carthaginian power grew, its impact on the indigenous population increased dramatically. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> civilization was already at a stage in which agriculture, manufacturing, trade, and political organization supported several states. Trade links between Carthage and the Berbers in the interior grew, but territorial expansion also resulted in the enslavement or military recruitment of some Berbers and in the extraction of tribute from others. By the early 4th century BC, Berbers formed one of the largest element, with Gauls, of the Carthaginian army.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Mercenary_War" title="Mercenary War">Mercenary War</a> (241-238 BC), a rebellion was instigated by mercenary soldiers of Carthage and African allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Berber soldiers participated after being unpaid following the defeat of Carthage in the <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a>. Berbers succeeded in obtaining control of much of Carthage's North African territory, and they minted coins bearing the name Libyan, used in Greek to describe natives of North Africa. The Carthaginian state declined because of successive defeats by the Romans in the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>; in 146 BC the city of Carthage was destroyed. As Carthaginian power waned, the influence of Berber leaders in the hinterland grew. By the 2nd century BC, several large but loosely administered Berber kingdoms had emerged. Two of them were established in <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a>, behind the coastal areas controlled by Carthage. West of Numidia lay Mauretania, which extended across the <a href="/wiki/Moulouya_River" title="Moulouya River">Moulouya River</a> in Morocco to the Atlantic Ocean. The high point of Berber civilization, unequaled until the coming of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohad" title="Almohad">Almohads</a> and <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid dynasty</a> more than a millennium later, was reached during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Masinissa" title="Masinissa">Masinissa</a> in the 2nd century BC. After Masinissa's death in 148 BC, the Berber kingdoms were divided and reunited several times. Masinissa's line survived until 24 AD, when the remaining Berber territory was annexed to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Macrobia_and_the_Barbari_City_States">Macrobia and the Barbari City States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Macrobia and the Barbari City States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobia" title="Macrobia">Macrobia</a></div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history_of_Somalia" title="Maritime history of Somalia">Maritime history of Somalia</a></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="476" data-file-width="788" decoding="async" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/200px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/300px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Herodotus_world_map-en.svg/400px-Herodotus_world_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herodotus_world_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oikumene" title="Oikumene">Oikumene</a> (inhabited world) as described by Herodotus in the 5th century BC.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qableh1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Qableh1.JPG/220px-Qableh1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Qableh1.JPG/330px-Qableh1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Qableh1.JPG/440px-Qableh1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qableh1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ruins of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qa%27ableh" title="Qa'ableh">Qa'ableh</a>, an early center of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people">Somali</a> civilization</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobia" title="Macrobia">Macrobia</a> was an ancient kingdom situated in the Horn of Africa (Present day Somalia) it is mentioned in the 5th century BC. According to Herodotus' account, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Emperor" title="Persian Emperor">Persian Emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Cambyses_II" title="Cambyses II">Cambyses II</a> upon his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Achaemenid_Egypt" title="History of Achaemenid Egypt">conquest of Egypt</a> (525 BC) sent ambassadors to Macrobia, bringing luxury gifts for the Macrobian king to entice his submission. The Macrobian ruler, who was elected based at least in part on stature, replied instead with a challenge for his Persian counterpart in the form of an unstrung <a href="/wiki/Bow_and_arrow" title="Bow and arrow">bow</a>: if the Persians could manage to string it, they would have the right to invade his country; but until then, they should thank the gods that the Macrobians never decided to invade their empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitto2_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kitto2-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Macrobians" title="Macrobians">Macrobians</a> were a regional power reputed for their advanced architecture and gold wealth, which was so plentiful that they shackled their prisoners in golden chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitto2_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kitto2-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the collapse of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macrobia" title="Macrobia">Macrobia</a>, several wealthy ancient city-states, such as <a href="/wiki/Opone" title="Opone">Opone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essina" title="Essina">Essina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sarapion" title="Sarapion">Sarapion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nikon_(Somalia)" title="Nikon (Somalia)">Nikon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malao_(ancient)" title="Malao (ancient)">Malao</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damo,_Somalia" title="Damo, Somalia">Damo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosylon" title="Mosylon">Mosylon</a> near <a href="/wiki/Cape_Guardafui" title="Cape Guardafui">Cape Guardafui</a> would emerge from the 1st millennium BC–500 AD to compete with the <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaeans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthians</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" title="Aksumite Empire">Axumites</a> for the wealthy <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">Indo</a>-<a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a> trade and flourished along the Somali coast. They developed a lucrative trading network under a region collectively known in the Peripilus of the Erythraean Sea as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbaria_(East_Africa)" title="Barbaria (East Africa)">Barbaria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_North_Africa">Roman North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Roman North Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)#History" title="Africa (Roman province)">Africa (Roman province) § History</a>, <a href="/wiki/Numidia#History" title="Numidia">Numidia § History</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya_in_the_Roman_era#History" title="Libya in the Roman era">Libya in the Roman era § History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romans_in_Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Romans in Sub-Saharan Africa">Romans in Sub-Saharan Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Africa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2439" data-file-width="3057" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman_Africa.JPG/220px-Roman_Africa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman_Africa.JPG/330px-Roman_Africa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Roman_Africa.JPG/440px-Roman_Africa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_Africa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern Africa under Roman rule</div></div></div>
<p>"Increases in <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> and in the area under cultivation during Roman rule caused wholesale dislocations of the Berber society, forcing nomad tribes to settle or to move from their traditional rangelands. Sedentary tribes lost their autonomy and connection with the land. Berber opposition to the Roman presence was nearly constant. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> established a frontier in the south by encircling the Aurès and <a href="/wiki/Nemencha" title="Nemencha">Nemencha</a> mountains and building a line of forts from Vescera (modern <a href="/wiki/Biskra" title="Biskra">Biskra</a>) to Ad Majores (Henchir Besseriani,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> southeast of Biskra). The defensive line extended at least as far as Castellum Dimmidi (modern <a href="/wiki/Messa%C3%A2d" title="Messaâd">Messaâd</a>, southwest of Biskra), Roman Algeria's southernmost fort. Romans settled and developed the area around Sitifis (modern <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9tif" title="Sétif">Sétif</a>) in the 2nd century, but farther west the influence of Rome did not extend beyond the coast and principal military roads until much later."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Mummy_of_Demetrios,_95-100_C.E.,11.600.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1296" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg/150px-The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg/225px-The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg/300px-The_Mummy_of_Demetrios%2C_95-100_C.E.%2C11.600.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Mummy_of_Demetrios,_95-100_C.E.,11.600.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fayum_mummy_portraits" title="Fayum mummy portraits">Fayum mummy portrait</a> of Demetrios, a citizen of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 100 AD</span>, <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum" title="Brooklyn Museum">Brooklyn Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Roman military presence of North Africa remained relatively small, consisting of about 28,000 troops and auxiliaries in <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> and the two <a href="/wiki/Mauretania" title="Mauretania">Mauretanian</a> provinces. Starting in the 2nd century AD, these garrisons were manned mostly by local inhabitants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>, urbanization in North Africa came in part with the establishment of settlements of veterans under the Roman emperors <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> (reigned 41–54), <a href="/wiki/Nerva" title="Nerva">Nerva</a> (96–98), and <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> (98–117). In Algeria such settlements included <a href="/wiki/Tipasa" title="Tipasa">Tipasa</a>, Cuicul or Curculum (modern <a href="/wiki/Dj%C3%A9mila" title="Djémila">Djemila</a>, northeast of Sétif), Thamugadi (modern <a href="/wiki/Timgad" title="Timgad">Timgad</a>, southeast of Sétif), and Sitifis (modern Sétif). The prosperity of most towns depended on <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. Called the "granary of the empire", North Africa became one of the largest exporters of grain in the empire, shipping to the provinces which did not produce cereals, like <a href="/wiki/Roman_Italy" title="Roman Italy">Italy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Greece" title="Roman Greece">Greece</a>. Other crops included fruit, figs, grapes, and beans. By the 2nd century AD, olive oil rivaled cereals as an export item.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The beginnings of the <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Historiography of the fall of the Western Roman Empire">Roman imperial decline</a> seemed less serious in North Africa than elsewhere. However, uprisings did take place. In 238 AD, landowners rebelled unsuccessfully against imperial fiscal policies. Sporadic tribal revolts in the Mauretanian mountains followed from 253 to 288, during the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_of_the_Third_Century" title="Crisis of the Third Century">Crisis of the Third Century</a>. The towns also suffered economic difficulties, and building activity almost ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>The towns of Roman North Africa had a substantial <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_the_Roman_Empire" title="History of the Jews in the Roman Empire">Jewish population</a>. Some Jews had been deported from <a href="/wiki/Judea" title="Judea">Judea</a> or <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> in the 1st and 2nd centuries AD for rebelling against Roman rule; others had come earlier with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punics" title="Punics">Punic</a> settlers. In addition, a number of Berber tribes had converted to <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:357px;max-width:357px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:148px;max-width:148px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:215px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Memnon_the_Ethiopian_(detail).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2806" data-file-width="1903" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg/146px-Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg/219px-Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg/292px-Memnon_the_Ethiopian_%28detail%29.jpg 2x" width="146"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:205px;max-width:205px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:215px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1798" data-file-width="1699" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg/203px-Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg/305px-Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg/406px-Antakya_Arkeoloji_Muzesi_1250320_nevit_cr.jpg 2x" width="203"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><b>Left</b>: Memnon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foster_child" title="Foster child">foster child</a> of <a href="/wiki/Herodes_Atticus" title="Herodes Atticus">Herodes Atticus</a>; marble bust (showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan" title="Sub-Saharan">sub-Saharan</a> facial features), ca. 170 AD, from the villa of Herodes Atticus at <a href="/wiki/Eva,_Arcadia" title="Eva, Arcadia">Eva, Arcadia</a>.<br/><b>Right</b>: an ancient <a href="/wiki/Roman_mosaic" title="Roman mosaic">Roman mosaic</a> from <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> depicting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan" title="Sub-Saharan">sub-Saharan</a> African man carrying goods over his shoulder.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Christianity arrived in the 2nd century and soon gained converts in the towns and among slaves. More than eighty bishops, some from distant frontier regions of Numidia, attended the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Carthage_(256)" title="Council of Carthage (256)">Council of Carthage (256)</a> in 256. By the end of the 4th century, the settled areas had become <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianized</a>, and some Berber tribes had converted <i>en masse</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>A division in the church that came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Donatism" title="Donatism">Donatist</a> <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> began in 313 among <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> in North Africa. The Donatists stressed the holiness of the church and refused to accept the authority to administer the sacraments of those who had surrendered the scriptures when they were forbidden under the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Diocletian" title="Diocletian">Diocletian</a> (reigned 284–305). The Donatists also opposed the involvement of <a href="/wiki/Constantine_the_Great" title="Constantine the Great">Constantine the Great</a> (reigned 306–337) in church affairs in contrast to the majority of Christians who welcomed official imperial recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>The occasionally violent Donatist controversy has been characterized<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2013)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> as a struggle between opponents and supporters of the Roman system. The most articulate North African critic of the Donatist position, which came to be called a heresy, was <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine</a>, bishop of <a href="/wiki/Hippo_Regius" title="Hippo Regius">Hippo Regius</a>. Augustine maintained that the unworthiness of a minister did not affect the validity of the sacraments because their true minister was <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>. In his sermons and books Augustine, who is considered a leading exponent of Christian dogma, evolved a theory of the right of orthodox Christian rulers to use force against schismatics and heretics. Although the dispute was resolved by a decision of an imperial commission in Carthage in 411, Donatist communities continued to exist as late as the 6th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>A decline in trade weakened Roman control. Independent kingdoms emerged in mountainous and desert areas, towns were overrun, and Berbers, who had previously been pushed to the edges of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, returned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Vandalic_War" title="Vandalic War">Vandalic War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Belisarius" title="Belisarius">Belisarius</a>, general of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a> based in <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, landed in North Africa in 533 with 16,000 men and within a year destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Vandal_Kingdom" title="Vandal Kingdom">Vandal Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Local opposition delayed full Byzantine control of the region for twelve years, however, and when imperial control came, it was but a shadow of the control exercised by Rome. Although an impressive series of fortifications were built, Byzantine rule was compromised by official corruption, incompetence, military weakness, and lack of concern in Constantinople for African affairs, which made it an easy target for the Arabs during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">Early Muslim conquests</a>. As a result, many rural areas reverted to Berber rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aksum">Aksum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Aksum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum#History" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Kingdom of Aksum § History</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ethiopia" title="History of Ethiopia">History of Ethiopia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_historiography" title="Ethiopian historiography">Ethiopian historiography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LocationAksumiteEmpire.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="295" data-file-width="397" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/LocationAksumiteEmpire.png/220px-LocationAksumiteEmpire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/LocationAksumiteEmpire.png/330px-LocationAksumiteEmpire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/LocationAksumiteEmpire.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LocationAksumiteEmpire.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aksumite Empire</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4597" data-file-width="3678" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg/180px-ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg/270px-ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg/360px-ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ET_Axum_asv2018-01_img37_Stelae_Park.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksum_Obelisk" title="Aksum Obelisk">Aksum Obelisk</a>, symbol of the Aksumite civilization</div></div></div>
<p>The earliest state in <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, <a href="/wiki/D%CA%BFmt" title="Dʿmt">Dʿmt</a>, dates from around the 8th and 7th centuries BC. D'mt traded through the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> with Egypt and the Mediterranean, providing frankincense. By the 5th and 3rd centuries, D'mt had declined, and several successor states took its place. Later there was greater trade with <a href="/wiki/South_Arabia" title="South Arabia">South Arabia</a>, mainly with the port of <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Saba</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adulis" title="Adulis">Adulis</a> became an important commercial center in the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Highlands" title="Ethiopian Highlands">Ethiopian Highlands</a>. The interaction of the peoples in the two regions, the southern Arabia <a href="/wiki/Sabaeans" title="Sabaeans">Sabaeans</a> and the northern Ethiopians, resulted in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez" title="Ge'ez">Ge'ez</a> culture and language and eventual development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_script" title="Ge'ez script">Ge'ez script</a>. Trade links increased and expanded from the Red Sea to the Mediterranean, with Egypt, Israel, Phoenicia, Greece, and Rome, to the Black Sea, and to Persia, India, and China. Aksum was known throughout those lands. By the 5th century BC, the region was very prosperous, exporting ivory, hippopotamus hides, gold dust, spices, and live elephants. It imported silver, gold, olive oil, and wine. Aksum manufactured glass crystal, brass, and copper for export. A powerful Aksum emerged, unifying parts of eastern Sudan, northern Ethiopia (<a href="/wiki/Tigray_Region" title="Tigray Region">Tigre</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a>. Its kings built stone palatial buildings and were buried under megalithic monuments. By 300 AD, Aksum was minting its own coins in silver and gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 331 AD, King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ezana" title="Ezana">Ezana</a> (320–350 AD) was converted to <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Tewahedo" title="Orthodox Tewahedo">Miaphysite Christianity</a> which believes in one united divine-human nature of Christ, supposedly by <a href="/wiki/Frumentius" title="Frumentius">Frumentius</a> and Aedesius, who became stranded on the Red Sea coast. Some scholars believed the process was more complex and gradual than a simple conversion. Around 350, the time Ezana sacked Meroe, the Syrian monastic tradition took root within the Ethiopian church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 6th century Aksum was powerful enough to add Saba on the Arabian peninsula to her empire. At the end of the 6th century, the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> pushed Aksum out of the peninsula. With the spread of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> through <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> and Northern Africa, Aksum's trading networks in the Mediterranean faltered. The Red Sea trade diminished as it was diverted to the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and dominated by <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, causing Aksum to decline. By 800 AD, the capital was moved south into the interior highlands, and Aksum was much diminished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Africa">West Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: West Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_West_Africa" title="History of West Africa">History of West Africa</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Serer_prehistory" title="Serer prehistory">Serer prehistory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Serer_history" title="Timeline of Serer history">Timeline of Serer history</a></div>
<p>In the western <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> the rise of settled communities occurred largely as a result of the domestication of <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a>. Archaeology points to sizable urban populations in West Africa beginning in the 2nd millennium BC. Symbiotic trade relations developed before the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">trans-Saharan trade</a>, in response to the opportunities afforded by north–south diversity in ecosystems across deserts, grasslands, and forests. The agriculturists received salt from the desert nomads. The desert nomads acquired meat and other foods from pastoralists and farmers of the grasslands and from fishermen on the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>. The forest-dwellers provided furs and meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="1660" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/130px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/195px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg/260px-Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nok_sculpture_Louvre_70-1998-11-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nok sculpture, terracotta, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dhar_Tichitt" title="Dhar Tichitt">Dhar Tichitt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oualata" title="Oualata">Oualata</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> figure prominently among the early urban centers, dated to 2,000 BC. About 500 stone settlements litter the region in the former savannah of the Sahara. Its inhabitants fished and grew millet. It has been found Augustin Holl that the <a href="/wiki/Soninke_people" title="Soninke people">Soninke</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mand%C3%A9_peoples" title="Mandé peoples">Mandé peoples</a> were likely responsible for constructing such settlements. Around 300 BC the region became more desiccated and the settlements began to decline, most likely relocating to <a href="/wiki/Koumbi_Saleh" title="Koumbi Saleh">Koumbi Saleh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HollA1985_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-HollA1985-134">[134]</a></sup> Architectural evidence and the comparison of pottery styles suggest that Dhar Tichitt was related to the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9-Djenno" title="Djenné-Djenno">Djenné-Djenno</a> (in present-day <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>) was settled around 300 BC, and the town grew to house a sizable <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> population, as evidenced by crowded cemeteries. Living structures were made of sun-dried mud. By 250 BC <a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9-Djenno" title="Djenné-Djenno">Djenné-Djenno</a> had become a large, thriving market town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Towns similar to that at Djenne-Jeno also developed at the site of <a href="/wiki/Dia,_Mali" title="Dia, Mali">Dia</a>, also in Mali along the Niger River, from around 900 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arazi_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arazi-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Farther south, in central <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, around 1,500 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Nok_culture" title="Nok culture">Nok culture</a> developed in <a href="/wiki/Jos_Plateau" title="Jos Plateau">Jos Plateau</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PB_2014_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-PB_2014-138">[138]</a></sup> It was a highly centralized community. The Nok people produced lifelike representations in <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a>, including human heads and human figures, elephants, and other animals. By 500 BC they were smelting iron. By 200 AD the Nok culture had vanished. Based on stylistic similarities with the Nok terracottas, the bronze figurines of the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a> kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ife" title="Ife">Ife</a> and those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bini_people" title="Bini people">Bini</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a> are now<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> to be continuations of the traditions of the earlier Nok culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bantu_expansion">Bantu expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Bantu expansion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples#History" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu peoples § History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion#Expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion § Expansion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bantu_Phillipson.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="332" decoding="async" height="451" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Bantu_Phillipson.png/220px-Bantu_Phillipson.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Bantu_Phillipson.png/330px-Bantu_Phillipson.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Bantu_Phillipson.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bantu_Phillipson.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>1</b> = 3000 – 1500 BC origin<br/><b>2</b> = c. 1500 BC first migrations<br/><span class="nowrap">    </span> <b>2.a</b> = Eastern Bantu,<br/><span class="nowrap">    </span> <b>2.b</b> = Western Bantu<br/><b>3</b> = 1000 – 500 BC <a href="/wiki/Urewe" title="Urewe">Urewe</a> nucleus of Eastern Bantu<br/><b>4</b> – <b>7</b> = southward advance<br/><b>9</b> = 500 BC – 0 Congo nucleus<br/><b>10</b> = 0 – 1000 CE last phase<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> involved a significant movement of people in African history and in the settling of the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> People speaking <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> (a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo family</a>) began in the second millennium BC to spread from <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> eastward to the <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">Great Lakes region</a>. In the first millennium BC, Bantu languages spread from the Great Lakes to southern and east Africa. One early movement headed south to the upper <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> valley in the 2nd century BC. Then Bantu-speakers pushed westward to the savannahs of present-day <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> and eastward into <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> in the 1st century AD. The second thrust from the Great Lakes was eastward, 2,000 years ago, expanding to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> coast, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>. The eastern group eventually met the southern migrants from the Great Lakes in <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>. Both groups continued southward, with eastern groups continuing to <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and reaching <a href="/wiki/Maputo" title="Maputo">Maputo</a> in the 2nd century AD, and expanding as far as <a href="/wiki/Durban" title="Durban">Durban</a>.
</p><p>By the later first millennium AD, the expansion had reached the <a href="/wiki/Great_Kei_River" title="Great Kei River">Great Kei River</a> in present-day <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">Sorghum</a>, a major Bantu crop, could not thrive under the winter rainfall of <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> and the western Cape. <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> people inhabited the remaining parts of southern Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (May 2018)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Medieval_and_Early_Modern_.286th_to_18th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_and_Early_Modern_(6th_to_18th_centuries)"><span class="anchor" id="500_to_1800"></span>Medieval and Early Modern (6th to 18th centuries)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medieval and Early Modern (6th to 18th centuries)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sao_civilization">Sao civilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sao civilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sao_civilisation#Origins_and_decline" title="Sao civilisation">Sao civilisation § Origins and decline</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sao_civilization" title="Sao civilization">Sao civilization</a> flourished from about the sixth century BC to as late as the 16th century AD in Central Africa. The Sao lived by the <a href="/wiki/Chari_River" title="Chari River">Chari River</a> south of Lake Chad in territory that later became part of present-day <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>. They are the earliest people to have left clear traces of their presence in the territory of modern <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>. Today, several ethnic groups of northern Cameroon and southern Chad – but particularly the <a href="/wiki/Sara_people" title="Sara people">Sara people</a> – claim descent from the civilization of the Sao. Sao artifacts show that they were skilled workers in <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fanso_19_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fanso_19-143">[143]</a></sup> Finds include bronze sculptures and terracotta statues of human and animal figures, coins, funerary urns, household utensils, jewelry, highly decorated pottery, and spears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> The largest Sao archaeological finds have occurred south of Lake Chad.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kanem_Empire">Kanem Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Kanem Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire#History" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Kanem–Bornu Empire § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Borno_in_1810.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="280" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Borno_in_1810.svg/280px-Borno_in_1810.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Borno_in_1810.svg/420px-Borno_in_1810.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Borno_in_1810.svg/560px-Borno_in_1810.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Borno_in_1810.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Kanem and Bornu Empires in 1810</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem Empire</a> was centered in the <a href="/wiki/Chad_Basin" title="Chad Basin">Chad Basin</a>. It was known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem Empire</a> from the 9th century AD onward and lasted as the independent kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu</a> until 1893. At its height it encompassed an area covering not only much of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, but also parts of modern southern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>. The history of the Empire is mainly known from the Royal Chronicle or <i><a href="/wiki/Girgam" title="Girgam">Girgam</a></i> discovered in 1851 by the German traveller <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Barth" title="Heinrich Barth">Heinrich Barth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Kanem rose in the 8th century in the region to the north and east of Lake Chad. The Kanem empire went into decline, shrank, and in the 14th century was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Bilala_people" title="Bilala people">Bilala</a> invaders from the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Fitri" title="Lake Fitri">Lake Fitri</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalola200826_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalola200826-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Around the 9th century AD, the central Sudanic Empire of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem</a>, with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Njimi" title="Njimi">Njimi</a>, was founded by the <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri</a>-speaking nomads. Kanem arose by engaging in the trans-Saharan trade. It exchanged slaves captured by raiding the south for horses from North Africa, which in turn aided in the acquisition of slaves. By the late 11th century, the Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayfawa_Dynasty" title="Sayfawa Dynasty">Sayfawa (Saifawa) dynasty</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Hummay" title="Hummay">Humai (Hummay) ibn Salamna</a>. The Sayfawa Dynasty ruled for 771 years, making it one of the longest-lasting dynasties in human history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_2007_pp._88–89_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_2007_pp._88–89-147">[147]</a></sup> In addition to trade, taxation of local farms around Kanem became a source of state income. Kanem reached its peak under <i>Mai</i> (king) <a href="/wiki/Dunama_Dabbalemi" title="Dunama Dabbalemi">Dunama Dibalemi ibn Salma</a> (1210–1248). The empire reportedly was able to field 40,000 cavalry, and it extended from <a href="/wiki/Fezzan" title="Fezzan">Fezzan</a> in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Sao_civilisation" title="Sao civilisation">Sao</a> state in the south. <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> became firmly entrenched in the empire. Pilgrimages to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> were common; <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> had hostels set aside specifically for pilgrims from Kanem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bornu_Empire">Bornu Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Bornu Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire#Shift_of_the_Sayfuwa_court_from_Kanem_to_Bornu" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Kanem–Bornu Empire § Shift of the Sayfuwa court from Kanem to Bornu</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_people" title="Kanuri people">Kanuri people</a> led by the Sayfuwa migrated to the west and south of the lake, where they established the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a>. By the late 16th century the Bornu empire had expanded and recaptured the parts of Kanem that had been conquered by the Bulala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalola200827_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalola200827-150">[150]</a></sup> Satellite states of Bornu included the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Damagaram" title="Sultanate of Damagaram">Damagaram</a> in the west and Baguirmi to the southeast of Lake Chad.
Around 1400, the Sayfawa Dynasty moved its capital to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu</a>, a tributary state southwest of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> with a new capital <a href="/wiki/Ngazargamu" title="Ngazargamu">Birni Ngarzagamu</a>. Overgrazing had caused the pastures of Kanem to become too dry. In addition, political rivalry from the <a href="/wiki/Bilala_people" title="Bilala people">Bilala</a> clan was becoming intense. Moving to Bornu better situated the empire to exploit the trans-Saharan trade and to widen its network in that trade. Links to the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa</a> states were also established, providing horses and salt from <a href="/wiki/Bilma" title="Bilma">Bilma</a> for <a href="/wiki/Bono_state" title="Bono state">Bonoman</a> gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._183,_184_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._183,_184-151">[151]</a></sup> Mai <a href="/wiki/Ali_Gazi" title="Ali Gazi">Ali Gazi ibn Dunama</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1475</span> – 1503) defeated the Bilala, reestablishing complete control of Kanem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91-152">[152]</a></sup> During the early 16th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sayfawa_Dynasty" title="Sayfawa Dynasty">Sayfawa Dynasty</a> solidified its hold on the Bornu population after much rebellion. In the latter half of the 16th century, <i>Mai</i> <a href="/wiki/Idris_Alooma" title="Idris Alooma">Idris Alooma</a> modernized its military, in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a>. Turkish mercenaries were used to train the military. The Sayfawa Dynasty were the first monarchs south of the Sahara to import <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91-152">[152]</a></sup> The empire controlled all of the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> from the borders of <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> in the east to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hausaland" title="Hausaland">Hausaland</a> to the west. Friendly relationship was established with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> via <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a>. The <i>Mai</i> exchanged gifts with the <a href="/wiki/List_of_sultans_of_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="List of sultans of the Ottoman Empire">Ottoman sultan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson199196_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson199196-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CentralEastAfrica1750.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="574" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/CentralEastAfrica1750.png/280px-CentralEastAfrica1750.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/CentralEastAfrica1750.png/420px-CentralEastAfrica1750.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/CentralEastAfrica1750.png/560px-CentralEastAfrica1750.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CentralEastAfrica1750.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Major states of Middle Africa in 1750</div></div></div>
<p>During the 17th and 18th centuries, not much is known about Bornu. During the 18th century, it became a center of Islamic learning. However, Bornu's army became outdated by not importing new arms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._183,_184_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._183,_184-151">[151]</a></sup> and Kamembu had also begun its decline. The power of the <i>mai</i> was undermined by droughts and famine that were becoming more intense, internal rebellion in the pastoralist north, growing Hausa power, and the importation of firearms which made warfare more bloody. By 1841, the last <i>mai</i> was deposed, bringing to an end the long-lived Sayfawa Dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_and_Burns_2007,_p._91-152">[152]</a></sup> In its place, the <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Amin_al-Kanemi" title="Muhammad al-Amin al-Kanemi">al-Kanemi</a> dynasty of the <i>shehu</i> rose to power.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shilluk_Kingdom">Shilluk Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Shilluk Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shilluk_Kingdom#History" title="Shilluk Kingdom">Shilluk Kingdom § History</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Shilluk_Kingdom" title="Shilluk Kingdom">Shilluk Kingdom</a> was centered in <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> from the 15th century from along a strip of land along the western bank of the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Lake_No" title="Lake No">Lake No</a> to about 12° north <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a>. The capital and royal residence was in the town of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fashoda" title="Fashoda">Fashoda</a>. The kingdom was founded during the mid-15th century AD by its first ruler, Nyikang. During the 19th century, the Shilluk Kingdom faced decline following military assaults from the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and later British and Sudanese colonization in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baguirmi_Kingdom">Baguirmi Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Baguirmi Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Bagirmi#History" title="Sultanate of Bagirmi">Sultanate of Bagirmi § History</a></div>
<p>The Kingdom of Baguirmi existed as an independent state during the 16th and 17th centuries southeast of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chad" title="Lake Chad">Lake Chad</a> in what is now the country of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>. Baguirmi emerged to the southeast of the <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Kanem–Bornu Empire</a>. The kingdom's first ruler was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Baguirmi" title="List of rulers of Baguirmi">Mbang</a> Birni Besse. Later in his reign, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> conquered the state and made it a tributary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9,_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="536" data-file-width="737" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9%2C_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_ville_d%27Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9,_vue_du_poste_Fran%C3%A7ais_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ab%C3%A9ch%C3%A9" title="Abéché">Abéché</a>, capital of Wadai, in 1918 after the French had taken over</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wadai_Empire">Wadai Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Wadai Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wadai_Empire#Origins" title="Wadai Empire">Wadai Empire § Origins</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Wadai_Empire" title="Wadai Empire">Wadai Empire</a> was centered on <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a> from the 17th century. The <a href="/wiki/Tunjur_people" title="Tunjur people">Tunjur people</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Wadai_Empire" title="Wadai Empire">Wadai Kingdom</a> to the east of Bornu in the 16th century. In the 17th century there was a revolt of the <a href="/wiki/Maba_people" title="Maba people">Maba people</a> who established a Muslim dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>At first Wadai paid tribute to Bornu and Durfur, but by the 18th century Wadai was fully independent and had become an aggressor against its neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAppiahGates2010254_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAppiahGates2010254-75">[75]</a></sup>To the west of Bornu, by the 15th century the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kano" title="Kingdom of Kano">Kingdom of Kano</a> had become the most powerful of the <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa Kingdoms</a>, in an unstable truce with the Kingdom of Katsina to the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFalola200847_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFalola200847-157">[157]</a></sup> Both were absorbed into the <a href="/wiki/Sokoto_Caliphate" title="Sokoto Caliphate">Sokoto Caliphate</a> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fulani_Jihad" title="Fulani Jihad">Fulani Jihad</a> of 1805, which threatened Bornu itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUdo1970178_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUdo1970178-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Luba_Empire">Luba Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Luba Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Luba#Origins_and_foundation" title="Kingdom of Luba">Kingdom of Luba § Origins and foundation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luba_pottery-1908.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="665" data-file-width="465" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Luba_pottery-1908.jpg/130px-Luba_pottery-1908.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Luba_pottery-1908.jpg/195px-Luba_pottery-1908.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Luba_pottery-1908.jpg/260px-Luba_pottery-1908.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luba_pottery-1908.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Luba pottery</div></div></div>
<p>Sometime between 1300 and 1400 AD, <a href="/wiki/Kongolo_Mwamba" title="Kongolo Mwamba">Kongolo Mwamba</a> (Nkongolo) from the Balopwe clan unified the various <a href="/wiki/Luba_people" title="Luba people">Luba peoples</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kisale" title="Lake Kisale">Lake Kisale</a>. He founded the Kongolo Dynasty, which was later ousted by <a href="/wiki/Kalala_Ilunga" title="Kalala Ilunga">Kalala Ilunga</a>. Kalala expanded the kingdom west of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kisale" title="Lake Kisale">Lake Kisale</a>. A new centralized political system of spiritual kings (<span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">balopwe</i></span>) with a court council of head governors and sub-heads all the way to village heads. The <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">balopwe</i></span> was the direct communicator with the ancestral spirits and chosen by them. Conquered states were integrated into the system and represented in the court, with their titles. The authority of the <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">balopwe</i></span> resided in his spiritual power rather than his military authority. The army was relatively small. The Luba was able to control regional trade and collect tribute for redistribution. Numerous offshoot states were formed with founders claiming descent from the Luba. The Luba political system spread throughout <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malawi" title="Malawi">Malawi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, and the western Congo. Two major empires claiming Luba descent were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunda_Empire" title="Lunda Empire">Lunda Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maravi" title="Maravi">Maravi Empire</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bemba_people" title="Bemba people">Bemba people</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basimba_people" title="Basimba people">Basimba people</a> of northern Zambia were descended from Luba migrants who arrived in Zambia during the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991159–160_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991159–160-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lunda_Empire">Lunda Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Lunda Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Lunda#Origin" title="Kingdom of Lunda">Kingdom of Lunda § Origin</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_the_Lunda_Empire" title="List of rulers of the Lunda Empire">List of rulers of the Lunda Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lunda_houses-1854.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="537" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/170px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/255px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Lunda_houses-1854.jpg/340px-Lunda_houses-1854.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lunda_houses-1854.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lunda town and dwelling</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1450s, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luba_Empire" title="Luba Empire">Luba</a> from the royal family <a href="/wiki/Ilunga_Tshibinda" title="Ilunga Tshibinda">Ilunga Tshibinda</a> married <a href="/wiki/Lunda_people" title="Lunda people">Lunda</a> queen Rweej and united all Lunda peoples. Their son <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mulopwe</i></span> Luseeng expanded the kingdom. His son Naweej expanded the empire further and is known as the first Lunda emperor, with the title <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mwato yamvo</i></span> (<span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mwaant yaav</i></span>, <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mwant yav</i></span>), the Lord of Vipers. The Luba political system was retained, and conquered peoples were integrated into the system. The <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mwato yamvo</i></span> assigned a <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">cilool</i></span> or <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">kilolo</i></span> (royal adviser) and tax collector to each state conquered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991161_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991161-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Numerous states claimed descent from the Lunda. The <a href="/wiki/Imbangala" title="Imbangala">Imbangala</a> of inland <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> claimed descent from a founder, Kinguri, brother of Queen Rweej, who could not tolerate the rule of <span title="Luba-Katanga-language romanization"><i lang="lu-Latn">mulopwe</i></span> Tshibunda. <i>Kinguri</i> became the title of kings of states founded by Queen Rweej's brother. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luena_people" title="Luena people">Luena</a> (Lwena) and <a href="/wiki/Lozi_people" title="Lozi people">Lozi</a> (Luyani) in <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> also claim descent from Kinguri. During the 17th century, a Lunda chief and warrior called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mwata_Kazembe" title="Mwata Kazembe">Mwata Kazembe</a> set up an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Lunda" title="Eastern Lunda">Eastern Lunda</a> kingdom in the valley of the <a href="/wiki/Luapula_River" title="Luapula River">Luapula River</a>. The Lunda's western expansion also saw claims of descent by the <a href="/wiki/Yaka_people" title="Yaka people">Yaka</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pende_people" title="Pende people">Pende</a>. The Lunda linked Central Africa with the western coast trade. The kingdom of Lunda came to an end in the 19th century when it was invaded by the <a href="/wiki/Chokwe_people" title="Chokwe people">Chokwe</a>, who were armed with guns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991161_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991161-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Kongo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo#History" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KingdomKongo1711.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="774" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/KingdomKongo1711.png/280px-KingdomKongo1711.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/KingdomKongo1711.png/420px-KingdomKongo1711.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/KingdomKongo1711.png/560px-KingdomKongo1711.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KingdomKongo1711.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kongo in 1711</div></div></div>
<p>By the 15th century AD, the farming <a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">Bakongo people</a> (<i>ba</i> being the plural prefix) were unified as the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kongo" title="Kingdom of Kongo">Kingdom of Kongo</a> under a ruler called the <i><a href="/wiki/Manikongo" title="Manikongo">manikongo</a></i>, residing in the fertile <a href="/wiki/Pool_Malebo" title="Pool Malebo">Pool Malebo</a> area on the lower <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">Congo River</a>. The capital was <a href="/wiki/M%27banza-Kongo" title="M'banza-Kongo">M'banza-Kongo</a>. With superior organization, they were able to conquer their neighbors and extract tribute.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They were experts in metalwork, pottery, and weaving raffia cloth. They stimulated interregional trade via a tribute system controlled by the <i>manikongo</i>. Later, <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> (corn) and <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> (manioc) would be introduced to the region via trade with the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> at their ports at <a href="/wiki/Luanda" title="Luanda">Luanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benguela" title="Benguela">Benguela</a>. The maize and cassava would result in population growth in the region and other parts of Africa, replacing <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> as a main staple.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 16th century, the <i>manikongo</i> held authority from the Atlantic in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Kwango_River" title="Kwango River">Kwango River</a> in the east. Each territory was assigned a <i>mani-mpembe</i> (provincial governor) by the <i>manikongo</i>. In 1506, <a href="/wiki/Afonso_I_of_Kongo" title="Afonso I of Kongo">Afonso I</a> (1506–1542), a Christian, took over the throne. Slave trading increased with Afonso's wars of conquest. About 1568 to 1569, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaga_(people)" title="Jaga (people)">Jaga</a> invaded Kongo, laying waste to the kingdom and forcing the <i>manikongo</i> into exile. In 1574, Manikongo <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_I_of_Kongo" title="Álvaro I of Kongo">Álvaro I</a> was reinstated with the help of Portuguese mercenaries. During the latter part of the 1660s, the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portuguese</a> tried to gain control of Kongo. Manikongo <a href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_I_of_Kongo" title="António I of Kongo">António I</a> (1661–1665), with a Kongolese army of 5,000, was destroyed by an army of Afro-Portuguese at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mbwila" title="Battle of Mbwila">Battle of Mbwila</a>. The empire dissolved into petty polities, fighting among each other for war captives to sell into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991156–157_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991156–157-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p>Kongo gained captives from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ndongo" title="Kingdom of Ndongo">Kingdom of Ndongo</a> in wars of conquest. Ndongo was ruled by the <i>ngola</i>. Ndongo would also engage in slave trading with the Portuguese, with <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9" title="São Tomé">São Tomé</a> being a transit point to Brazil. The kingdom was not as welcoming as Kongo; it viewed the Portuguese with great suspicion and as an enemy.
