The Portuguese in the latter part of the 16th century tried to gain control of Ndongo but were defeated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Mbundu_people" title="Northern Mbundu people">Mbundu</a>. Ndongo experienced depopulation from slave raiding. The leaders established another state at <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Matamba" title="Kingdom of Matamba">Matamba</a>, affiliated with <a href="/wiki/Nzinga_of_Ndongo_and_Matamba" title="Nzinga of Ndongo and Matamba">Queen Nzinga</a>, who put up a strong resistance to the Portuguese until coming to terms with them. The Portuguese settled along the coast as trade dealers, not venturing on conquest of the interior. Slavery wreaked havoc in the interior, with states initiating wars of conquest for captives. The <a href="/wiki/Imbangala" title="Imbangala">Imbangala</a> formed the slave-raiding state of <a href="/wiki/Kasanje_Kingdom" title="Kasanje Kingdom">Kasanje</a>, a major source of slaves during the 17th and 18th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991158_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991158-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horn_of_Africa">Horn of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Horn of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa#History" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa § History</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Somalia">Somalia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Somalia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Somalia" title="History of Somalia">History of Somalia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gondereshe2008.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Gondereshe2008.jpg/220px-Gondereshe2008.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Gondereshe2008.jpg/330px-Gondereshe2008.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Gondereshe2008.jpg/440px-Gondereshe2008.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gondereshe2008.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Citadel" title="Citadel">Citadel</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gondershe" title="Gondershe">Gondershe, Somalia</a> was an important city in the medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people">Somali</a> <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Empire</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islam" title="History of Islam">birth of Islam</a> opposite Somalia's Red Sea coast meant that Somali merchants and sailors living on the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> gradually came under the influence of the new religion through their converted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> Muslim trading partners. With the migration of Muslim families from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a> to Somalia in the early centuries of Islam, and the peaceful conversion of the Somali population by <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Somalia" title="Islam in Somalia">Somali Muslim scholars</a> in the following centuries, the ancient city-states eventually transformed into Islamic <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berbera" title="Berbera">Berbera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zeila" title="Zeila">Zeila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Barawa</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merka" title="Merka">Merka</a>, which were part of the <i>Berber</i> (the medieval Arab term for the ancestors of the modern Somalis) civilization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laitin_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laitin-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The city of Mogadishu came to be known as the <i>City of Islam</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> and controlled the East African gold trade for several centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ancient-Almnara.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="650" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ancient-Almnara.jpg/180px-Ancient-Almnara.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ancient-Almnara.jpg/270px-Ancient-Almnara.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ancient-Almnara.jpg/360px-Ancient-Almnara.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ancient-Almnara.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Almnara Tower</i>, Mogadishu.</div></div></div>
<p>During this period, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanates" title="Sultanates">sultanates</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ajuran_Sultanate" title="Ajuran Sultanate">Ajuran Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Mogadishu" title="Sultanate of Mogadishu">Sultanate of Mogadishu</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republics" title="Republics">republics</a> like <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Barawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merca" title="Merca">Merca</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hobyo" title="Hobyo">Hobyo</a> and their respective ports flourished and had a lucrative foreign commerce with ships sailing to and coming from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, India, <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, Egypt, Portugal and as far away as China. <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>, who passed by Mogadishu in the 15th century, noted that it was a large city with houses four or five stories high and big palaces in its centre, in addition to many mosques with cylindrical minarets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Duarte_Barbosa" title="Duarte Barbosa">Duarte Barbosa</a> noted that many ships from the <a href="/wiki/Khambhat" title="Khambhat">Kingdom of Cambaya</a> in modern-day India sailed to Mogadishu with <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">cloth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spices" title="Spices">spices</a>, for which they in return received gold, wax, and ivory. Barbosa also highlighted the abundance of meat, wheat, barley, horses, and fruit in the coastal markets, which generated enormous wealth for the merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> Mogadishu, the center of a thriving weaving industry known as <i>toob benadir</i> (specialized for the markets in Egypt and Syria),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> together with <a href="/wiki/Merca" title="Merca">Merca</a> and Barawa, served as a transit stop for <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> merchants from <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> and for the gold trade from <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Sultanate" title="Kilwa Sultanate">Kilwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> Jewish merchants from the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Strait of Hormuz</a> brought their Indian textiles and fruit to the Somali coast to exchange for grain and wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trading relations were established with <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a> in the 15th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> with cloth, <a href="/wiki/Ambergris" title="Ambergris">ambergris</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> being the main commodities of the trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Giraffes, zebras, and incense were exported to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Empire" title="Ming Empire">Ming Empire</a> of China, which established Somali merchants as leaders in the commerce between the Asia and Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> and influenced the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese language</a> with borrowings from the <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali language</a> in the process. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> merchants from <a href="/wiki/Surat" title="Surat">Surat</a> and southeast African merchants from <a href="/wiki/Pate_Island" title="Pate Island">Pate</a>, seeking to bypass both the Portuguese blockade and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omani" title="Omani">Omani</a> meddling, used the Somali ports of Merca and Barawa (which were out of the two powers' jurisdiction) to conduct their trade in safety and without any problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Ethiopia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ethiopia" title="History of Ethiopia">History of Ethiopia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_historiography" title="Ethiopian historiography">Ethiopian historiography</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Zagwe_dynasty" title="Zagwe dynasty">Zagwe dynasty</a> ruled many parts of modern Ethiopia and Eritrea from approximately 1137 to 1270. The name of the dynasty comes from the <a href="/wiki/Cushitic_languages" title="Cushitic languages">Cushitic speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Agaw_people" title="Agaw people">Agaw</a> of northern Ethiopia. From 1270 AD and on for many centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_dynasty" title="Solomonic dynasty">Solomonic dynasty</a> ruled the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4243" data-file-width="5304" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg/220px-ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg/330px-ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg/440px-ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ET_Gondar_asv2018-02_img03_Fasil_Ghebbi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasilides_of_Ethiopia" title="Fasilides of Ethiopia">King Fasilides's Castle</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the early 15th century Ethiopia sought to make diplomatic contact with European kingdoms for the first time since Aksumite times. A letter from King <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry IV of England</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Ethiopia" title="Emperor of Ethiopia">Emperor of Abyssinia</a> survives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> In 1428, the Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yeshaq_I_of_Ethiopia" title="Yeshaq I of Ethiopia">Yeshaq I</a> sent two emissaries to <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_V_of_Aragon" title="Alfonso V of Aragon">Alfonso V of Aragon</a>, who sent return emissaries who failed to complete the return trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first continuous relations with a European country began in 1508 with the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Kingdom of Portugal</a> under Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dawit_II_of_Ethiopia" title="Dawit II of Ethiopia">Lebna Dengel</a>, who had just inherited the throne from his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> This proved to be an important development, for when the empire was subjected to the attacks of the <a href="/wiki/Adal_Sultanate" title="Adal Sultanate">Adal</a> general and <a href="/wiki/Imam" title="Imam">imam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_ibn_Ibrahim_al-Ghazi" title="Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi">Ahmad ibn Ibrahim al-Ghazi</a> (called "<i>Grañ</i>", or "the Left-handed"), Portugal assisted the Ethiopian emperor by sending weapons and four hundred men, who helped his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gelawdewos_of_Ethiopia" title="Gelawdewos of Ethiopia">Gelawdewos</a> defeat Ahmad and re-establish his rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abyssinian%E2%80%93Adal_War" title="Abyssinian–Adal War">Abyssinian–Adal War</a> was also one of the first <a href="/wiki/Proxy_war" title="Proxy war">proxy wars</a> in the region as the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, and Portugal took sides in the conflict.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>When Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Susenyos_of_Ethiopia" title="Susenyos of Ethiopia">Susenyos</a> converted to <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholicism</a> in 1624, years of revolt and civil unrest followed resulting in thousands of deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> missionaries had offended the Orthodox faith of the local Ethiopians, and on June 25, 1632, Susenyos's son, Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasilides_of_Ethiopia" title="Fasilides of Ethiopia">Fasilides</a>, declared the state religion to again be <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Christianity</a> and expelled the Jesuit missionaries and other Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Africa">North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: North Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_North_Africa" title="History of North Africa">History of North Africa</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Maghreb">Maghreb</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Maghreb">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maghreb#History" title="Maghreb">Maghreb § History</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Almohad_Caliphate" title="Almohad Caliphate">Almohad Caliphate</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saadi_dynasty" title="Saadi dynasty">Saadi dynasty</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaouite_dynasty" title="Alaouite dynasty">Alaouite dynasty</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/280px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/420px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg/560px-Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan_Panorama_-_Grande_Mosqu%C3%A9e_de_Kairouan_Panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosque_of_Uqba" title="Mosque of Uqba">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> (also known as the Mosque of Uqba), first built in 670 by the Umayyad general Uqba Ibn Nafi, is the oldest and most prestigious mosque in the Maghreb and North Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> located in the city of <a href="/wiki/Kairouan" title="Kairouan">Kairouan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div></div></div>
<p>By 711 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> had conquered all of North Africa. By the 10th century, the majority of the population of North Africa was Muslim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005_pp._65–67_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005_pp._65–67-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 9th century AD, the unity brought about by the Islamic conquest of North Africa and the expansion of Islamic culture came to an end. Conflict arose as to who should be the successor of the prophet. The <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyads</a> had initially taken control of the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a>, with their capital at <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>. Later, the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasids</a> had taken control, moving the capital to <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber people</a>, being independent in spirit and hostile to outside interference in their affairs and to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> exclusivity in orthodox Islam, adopted <a href="/wiki/Shia_Islam" title="Shia Islam">Shi'ite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kharijites" title="Kharijites">Kharijite</a> Islam, both considered unorthodox and hostile to the authority of the Abbasid Caliphate. Numerous Kharijite kingdoms came and fell during the 8th and 9th centuries, asserting their independence from Baghdad. In the early 10th century, Shi'ite groups from Syria, claiming descent from Muhammad's daughter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimah" title="Fatimah">Fatimah</a>, founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatimid_Dynasty" title="Fatimid Dynasty">Fatimid Dynasty</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>. By 950, they had conquered all of the Maghreb and by 969 all of Egypt. They had immediately broken away from Baghdad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>In an attempt to bring about a purer form of Islam among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zenaga_people" title="Zenaga people">Sanhaja</a> Berbers, <a href="/wiki/Abdallah_ibn_Yasin" title="Abdallah ibn Yasin">Abdallah ibn Yasin</a> founded the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid</a> movement in present-day <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>. The Sanhaja Berbers, like the <a href="/wiki/Soninke_people" title="Soninke people">Soninke</a>, practiced an indigenous religion alongside Islam. Abdallah ibn Yasin found ready converts in the <a href="/wiki/Lamtuna" title="Lamtuna">Lamtuna</a> Sanhaja, who were dominated by the Soninke in the south and the <a href="/wiki/Zenata" title="Zenata">Zenata</a> Berbers in the north. By the 1040s, all of the Lamtuna was converted to the Almoravid movement. With the help of Yahya ibn Umar and his brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu-Bakr_Ibn-Umar" title="Abu-Bakr Ibn-Umar">Abu Bakr ibn Umar</a>, the sons of the Lamtuna chief, the Almoravids created an empire extending from the Sahel to the Mediterranean. After the death of Abdallah ibn Yassin and Yahya ibn Umar, Abu Bakr split the empire in half, between himself and <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_ibn_Tashfin" title="Yusuf ibn Tashfin">Yusuf ibn Tashfin</a>, because it was too big to be ruled by one individual. Abu Bakr took the south to continue fighting the Soninke, and Yusuf ibn Tashfin took the north, expanding it to southern Spain. The death of Abu Bakr in 1087 saw a breakdown of unity and increase military dissension in the south. This caused a re-expansion of the Soninke. The Almoravids were once held responsible for bringing down the <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a> in 1076, but this view is no longer credited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 10th through 13th centuries, there was a large-scale movement of <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">bedouins</a> out of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>. About 1050, a quarter of a million Arab nomads from Egypt moved into the Maghreb. Those following the northern coast were referred to as <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a>. Those going south of the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> were the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Sulaym" title="Banu Sulaym">Banu Sulaym</a>. This movement spread the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> and hastened the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber language</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabisation</a> of North Africa. Later an Arabised Berber group, the Hawwara, went south to Nubia via Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Almohad1200.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="716" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/280px-Almohad1200.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/420px-Almohad1200.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Almohad1200.png/560px-Almohad1200.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Almohad1200.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Almohad Empire, c. 1200</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1140s, <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Mu%27min" title="Abd al-Mu'min">Abd al-Mu'min</a> declared <a href="/wiki/Jihad" title="Jihad">jihad</a> on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids">Almoravids</a>, charging them with decadence and corruption. He united the northern Berbers against the Almoravids, overthrowing them and forming the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Almohad_dynasty" title="Almohad dynasty">Almohad</a> Empire. During this period, the Maghreb became thoroughly <a href="/wiki/Islamization" title="Islamization">Islamised</a> and saw the spread of <a href="/wiki/Literacy" title="Literacy">literacy</a>, the development of <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>, and the use of the number <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/0_(number)" title="0 (number)">zero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimals</a>. By the 13th century, the Almohad states had split into three rival states. Muslim states were largely extinguished in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> by the Christian kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Castile" title="Kingdom of Castile">Castile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aragon" title="Kingdom of Aragon">Aragon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portugal</a>. Around 1415, Portugal engaged in a <i><a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">reconquista</a></i> of North Africa by capturing <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">Ceuta</a>, and in later centuries Spain and Portugal acquired other ports on the North African coast. In 1492, at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Granada_War" title="Granada War">Granada War</a>, Spain defeated Muslims in the <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">Emirate of Granada</a>, effectively ending eight centuries of Muslim domination in southern Iberia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Safi_minaret.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Safi_minaret.png/280px-Safi_minaret.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Safi_minaret.png/420px-Safi_minaret.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Safi_minaret.png/560px-Safi_minaret.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Safi_minaret.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Almohad minaret in Safi</div></div></div>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Portugal and Spain took the ports of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangiers" title="Tangiers">Tangiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>. This put them in direct competition with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, which re-took the ports using Turkish corsairs (<a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">pirates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Privateer" title="Privateer">privateers</a>). The Turkish corsairs would use the ports for raiding Christian ships, a major source of booty for the towns. Technically, North Africa was under the control of the Ottoman Empire, but only the coastal towns were fully under <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>'s control. Tripoli benefited from trade with <a href="/wiki/Borno_State" title="Borno State">Borno</a></span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashas" title="Pashas">pashas</a> of Tripoli traded horses, firearms, and armor via Fez with the sultans of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bornu_Empire" title="Bornu Empire">Bornu Empire</a> for slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 16th century, an Arab nomad tribe that claimed descent from Muhammad's daughter, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saadi_dynasty" title="Saadi dynasty">Saadis</a>, conquered and united <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>. They prevented the Ottoman Empire from reaching to the Atlantic and expelled Portugal from Morocco's western coast. <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_al-Mansur" title="Ahmad al-Mansur">Ahmad al-Mansur</a> brought the state to the height of its power. He invaded <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhay</a> in 1591, to control the gold trade, which had been diverted to the western coast of Africa for European ships and to the east, to Tunis. Morocco's hold on Songhay diminished in the 17th century. In 1603, after Ahmad's death, the kingdom split into the two sultanates of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fes" title="Fes">Fes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marrakesh" title="Marrakesh">Marrakesh</a>. Later it was reunited by <a href="/wiki/Al-Rashid_of_Morocco" title="Al-Rashid of Morocco">Moulay al-Rashid</a>, founder of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaouite_Dynasty" title="Alaouite Dynasty">Alaouite Dynasty</a> (1672–1727). His brother and successor, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ismail_ibn_Sharif" title="Ismail ibn Sharif">Ismail ibn Sharif</a> (1672–1727), strengthened the unity of the country by importing slaves from the Sudan to build up the military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nile_Valley">Nile Valley</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Nile Valley">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nile#History" title="Nile">Nile § History</a></div>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Egypt#Arab_and_Ottoman_Egypt_(641–1882)" title="History of Egypt">History of Egypt § Arab and Ottoman Egypt (641–1882)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_rulers_of_Egypt" title="Lists of rulers of Egypt">Lists of rulers of Egypt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="564" data-file-width="983" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/280px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/420px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG/560px-Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fatimid_Caliphate.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fatimid Caliphate</div></div></div>
<p>In 642 AD, the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a> conquered <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Byzantine Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005_pp._65–67_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005_pp._65–67-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt under the <a href="/wiki/Fatimid_Caliphate" title="Fatimid Caliphate">Fatimid Caliphate</a> was prosperous. Dams and canals were repaired, and wheat, barley, flax, and cotton production increased. Egypt became a major producer of linen and cotton cloth. Its Mediterranean and Red Sea trade increased. Egypt also minted a gold currency called the Fatimid dinar, which was used for international trade. The bulk of revenues came from taxing the <a href="/wiki/Fellah" title="Fellah">fellahin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peasant" title="Peasant">peasant</a> farmers), and taxes were high. Tax collecting was leased to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> overlords, who were soldiers who had taken part in the Fatimid conquest in 969 AD. The overlords paid a share to the caliphs and retained what was left. Eventually, they became landlords and constituted a settled land aristocracy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>To fill the military ranks, <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluk</a> Turkish slave cavalry and Sudanese slave infantry were used. Berber freemen were also recruited. In the 1150s, tax revenues from farms diminished. The soldiers revolted and wreaked havoc in the countryside, slowed trade, and diminished the power and authority of the Fatimid caliphs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1160s, Fatimid Egypt came under threat from <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">European crusaders</a>. Out of this threat, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> general named <a href="/wiki/Saladin" title="Saladin">Ṣalāḥ ad-Dīn Yūsuf ibn Ayyūb</a> (Saladin), with a small band of professional soldiers, emerged as an outstanding Muslim defender. Saladin defeated the Christian crusaders at Egypt's borders and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Jerusalem_(1187)" title="Siege of Jerusalem (1187)">recaptured Jerusalem</a> in 1187. On the death of <a href="/wiki/Al-Adid" title="Al-Adid">Al-Adid</a>, the last Fatimid caliph, in 1171, Saladin became the ruler of Egypt, ushering in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid_Dynasty" title="Ayyubid Dynasty">Ayyubid Dynasty</a>. Under his rule, Egypt returned to <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> became an important center of Arab Islamic learning, and Mamluk slaves were increasingly recruited from Turkey and southern Russia for military service. Support for the military was tied to the <i>iqta</i>, a form of land taxation in which soldiers were given ownership in return for military service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Over time, Mamluk slave soldiers became a very powerful <a href="/wiki/Landed_nobility" title="Landed nobility">landed aristocracy</a>, to the point of getting rid of the Ayyubid dynasty in 1250 and establishing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluk dynasty</a>. The more powerful Mamluks were referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Emir" title="Emir">amirs</a></i>. For 250 years, Mamluks controlled all of Egypt under a military dictatorship. Egypt extended her territories to Syria and Palestine, thwarted the crusaders, and halted a <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> invasion in 1260 at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ain_Jalut" title="Battle of Ain Jalut">Battle of Ain Jalut</a>. Mamluk Egypt came to be viewed as a protector of Islam, and of <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>. Eventually the <i>iqta</i> system declined and proved unreliable for providing an adequate military. The Mamluks started viewing their <i>iqta</i> as hereditary and became attuned to urban living. Farm production declined, and dams and canals lapsed into disrepair. Mamluk military skill and technology did not keep pace with new technology of <a href="/wiki/Handgun" title="Handgun">handguns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, Egypt was easily defeated. In 1517, at the end of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman%E2%80%93Mamluk_War_(1516%E2%80%9317)" title="Ottoman–Mamluk War (1516–17)">Ottoman–Mamluk War</a>, Egypt became part of the Ottoman Empire. The <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> government revived the <i>iqta</i> system. Trade was reestablished in the Red Sea, but it could not completely connect with the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> trade because of growing Portuguese presence. During the 17th and 18th centuries, hereditary Mamluks regained power. The leading Mamluks were referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Bey" title="Bey">beys</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pashas</a>, or viceroys, represented the Istanbul government in name only, operating independently. During the 18th century, dynasties of pashas became established. The government was weak and corrupt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1798, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> <a href="/wiki/French_campaign_in_Egypt_and_Syria" title="French campaign in Egypt and Syria">invaded Egypt</a>. The local forces had little ability to resist the French conquest. However, the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> and the Ottoman Empire were able to remove French occupation in 1801. These events marked the beginning of a 19th-century Anglo-Franco rivalry over Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Sudan">Sudan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Sudan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sudan" title="History of Sudan">History of Sudan</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_and_Islamic_Nubia">Christian and Islamic Nubia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Christian and Islamic Nubia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nubia#Christian_Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia § Christian Nubia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nubia#Islamic_Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia § Islamic Nubia</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Makuria" title="List of rulers of Makuria">List of rulers of Makuria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christian_Nubia.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="499" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/180px-Christian_Nubia.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/270px-Christian_Nubia.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Christian_Nubia.png/360px-Christian_Nubia.png 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christian_Nubia.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christian Nubia and the Nile <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a></div></div></div>
<p>After <a href="/wiki/Ezana_of_Axum" title="Ezana of Axum">Ezana of Aksum</a> sacked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meroe" title="Meroe">Meroe</a>, people associated with the site of <a href="/wiki/Ballana" title="Ballana">Ballana</a> moved into <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a> from the southwest and founded three kingdoms: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Makuria" title="Kingdom of Makuria">Makuria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nobatia" title="Nobatia">Nobatia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a>. They would rule for 200 years. Makuria was above the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">third cataract</a>, along the Dongola Reach with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Dongola" title="Dongola">Dongola</a>. Nobadia was to the north with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Faras" title="Faras">Faras</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a> was to the south with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Soba_(city)" title="Soba (city)">Soba</a>. Makuria eventually absorbed Nobadia. The people of the region converted to <a href="/wiki/Monophysitism" title="Monophysitism">Monophysite Christianity</a> around 500 to 600 CE. The church initially started writing in <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, then in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, and finally in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Nubian_language" title="Old Nubian language">Old Nubian</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nilo-Saharan_languages" title="Nilo-Saharan languages">Nilo-Saharan</a> language. The church was aligned with the <a href="/wiki/Coptic_Orthodox_Church" title="Coptic Orthodox Church">Egyptian Coptic Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 641, <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Egypt" title="Muslim conquest of Egypt">Egypt was conquered</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>. This effectively blocked Christian Nubia and Aksum from Mediterranean Christendom. In 651–652, Arabs from Egypt invaded Christian Nubia. Nubian archers soundly defeated the invaders. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bakt" title="Bakt">Baqt (or Bakt) Treaty</a> was drawn, recognizing Christian Nubia and regulating trade. The treaty controlled relations between Christian Nubia and Islamic Egypt for almost six hundred years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 13th century, Christian Nubia began its decline. The authority of the monarchy was diminished by the church and nobility. Arab <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">bedouin</a> tribes began to infiltrate Nubia, causing further havoc. <i><a href="/wiki/Fakir" title="Fakir">Fakirs</a></i> (holy men) practicing <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufism</a> introduced Islam into Nubia. By 1366, Nubia had become divided into petty fiefdoms when it was invaded by <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluks</a>. During the 15th century, Nubia was open to Arab immigration. Arab nomads intermingled with the population and introduced the <a href="/wiki/Arab_culture" title="Arab culture">Arab culture</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> language. By the 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Makuria" title="Makuria">Makuria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobadia" title="Nobadia">Nobadia</a> had been <a href="/wiki/Islamization" title="Islamization">Islamized</a>. During the 16th century, Abdallah Jamma headed an Arab confederation that destroyed Soba, capital of Alodia, the last holdout of Christian Nubia. Later Alodia would fall under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sennar_(sultanate)" title="Sennar (sultanate)">Funj Sultanate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 15th century, <a href="/wiki/Funj_people" title="Funj people">Funj</a> herders migrated north to <a href="/wiki/Alodia" title="Alodia">Alodia</a> and occupied it. Between 1504 and 1505, the kingdom expanded, reaching its peak and establishing its capital at <a href="/wiki/Sennar" title="Sennar">Sennar</a> under <a href="/wiki/Badi_II" title="Badi II">Badi II Abu Daqn</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1644</span> – 1680). By the end of the 16th century, the Funj had converted to Islam. They pushed their empire westward to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdufan" title="Kurdufan">Kordofan</a>. They expanded eastward, but were halted by Ethiopia. They controlled Nubia down to the 3rd Cataract. The economy depended on captured enemies to fill the army and on merchants travelling through Sennar. Under <a href="/wiki/Badi_IV" title="Badi IV">Badi IV</a> (1724–1762), the army turned on the king, making him nothing but a figurehead. In 1821, the Funj were conquered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_of_Egypt" title="Muhammad Ali of Egypt">Muhammad Ali</a> (1805–1849), Pasha of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPage200188_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPage200188-209">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELye2002189_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELye2002189-210">[210]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Africa">Southern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Southern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa#History" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa § History</a></div>
<p>Settlements of <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu-speaking</a> peoples who were iron-using agriculturists and herdsmen were long already well established south of the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo River</a> by the 4th century CE, displacing and absorbing the original <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan speakers</a>. They slowly moved south, and the earliest ironworks in modern-day <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal Province</a> are believed to date from around 1050.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag is ambiguous, and needs clarification. (March 2020)">ambiguous</span></a></i>]</sup> The southernmost group was the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa people</a>, whose language incorporates certain linguistic traits from the earlier Khoi-San people, reaching the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fish_River" title="Great Fish River">Great Fish River</a> in today's <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Cape" title="Eastern Cape">Eastern Cape Province</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Zimbabwe_and_Mapungubwe">Great Zimbabwe and Mapungubwe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Great Zimbabwe and Mapungubwe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe#Description" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe § Description</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mapungubwe#Origin" title="Kingdom of Mapungubwe">Kingdom of Mapungubwe § Origin</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Mutapa" title="List of rulers of Mutapa">List of rulers of Mutapa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tower,_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="982" data-file-width="697" decoding="async" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/180px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/270px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg/360px-Tower%2C_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tower,_Great_Zimbabwe1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Towers of <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mapungubwe" title="Kingdom of Mapungubwe">Kingdom of Mapungubwe</a> was the first state in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, with its capital at Mapungubwe. The state arose in the 12th century CE. Its wealth came from controlling the trade in ivory from the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo Valley</a>, copper from the mountains of northern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transvaal_Province" title="Transvaal Province">Transvaal</a>, and gold from the <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> Plateau between the Limpopo and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zambezi_River" title="Zambezi River">Zambezi</a> rivers, with the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> merchants at <a href="/wiki/Chibuene" title="Chibuene">Chibuene</a>. By the mid-13th century, Mapungubwe was abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the decline of Mapungubwe, <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> rose on the Zimbabwe Plateau. <i>Zimbabwe</i> means stone building. Great Zimbabwe was the first city in Southern Africa and was the center of an empire, consolidating lesser <a href="/wiki/Shona_people" title="Shona people">Shona</a> polities. Stone building was inherited from Mapungubwe. These building techniques were enhanced and came into maturity at Great Zimbabwe, represented by the wall of the Great Enclosure. The dry-stack stone masonry technology was also used to build smaller compounds in the area. Great Zimbabwe flourished by trading with Swahili Kilwa and <a href="/wiki/Sofala" title="Sofala">Sofala</a>. The rise of Great Zimbabwe parallels the rise of Kilwa. Great Zimbabwe was a major source of gold. Its royal court lived in luxury, wore Indian cotton, surrounded themselves with copper and gold ornaments, and ate on plates from as far away as Persia and China. Around the 1420s and 1430s, Great Zimbabwe was on decline. The city was abandoned by 1450. Some have attributed the decline to the rise of the trading town <a href="/wiki/Ingombe_Ilede" title="Ingombe Ilede">Ingombe Ilede</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._147–153_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._147–153-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>A new chapter of Shona history ensued. Nyatsimba Mutota, a northern Shona king of the Karanga, engaged in conquest. He and his son Mutope conquered the Zimbabwe Plateau, going through <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> to the east coast, linking the empire to the coastal trade. They called their empire <i>Wilayatu 'l Mu'anamutapah</i> or <i>mwanamutapa</i> (Lord of the Plundered Lands), or the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Kingdom of Mutapa</a>. <i>Monomotapa</i> was the Portuguese corruption. They did not build stone structures; the northern Shonas had no traditions of building in stone. After the death of Matope in 1480, the empire split into two small empires: <a href="/wiki/Torwa_dynasty" title="Torwa dynasty">Torwa</a> in the south and Mutapa in the north. The split occurred over rivalry from two Shona lords, Changa and Togwa, with the <i>mwanamutapa</i> line. Changa was able to acquire the south, forming the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Butua" title="Kingdom of Butua">Kingdom of Butua</a> with its capital at <a href="/wiki/Khami" title="Khami">Khami</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._147–153_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._147–153-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mutapa" title="Kingdom of Mutapa">Mutapa Empire</a> continued in the north under the <i>mwenemutapa</i> line. During the 16th century the Portuguese were able to establish permanent markets up the Zambezi River in an attempt to gain political and military control of Mutapa. They were partially successful. In 1628, a decisive battle allowed them to put a puppet <i>mwanamutapa</i> named Mavura, who signed treaties that gave favorable mineral export rights to the Portuguese. The Portuguese were successful in destroying the <i>mwanamutapa</i> system of government and undermining trade. By 1667, Mutapa was in decay. Chiefs would not allow digging for gold because of fear of Portuguese theft, and the population declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Butua" title="Kingdom of Butua">Kingdom of Butua</a> was ruled by a <i>changamire</i>, a title derived from the founder, Changa. Later it became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rozwi_Empire" title="Rozwi Empire">Rozwi Empire</a>. The Portuguese tried to gain a foothold but were thrown out of the region in 1693, by Changamire Dombo. The 17th century was a period of peace and prosperity. The Rozwi Empire fell into ruins in the 1830s from invading <a href="/wiki/Nguni_people" title="Nguni people">Nguni</a> from <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">Natal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254_215-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991252–254-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Namibia">Namibia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Namibia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Namibia" title="History of Namibia">History of Namibia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Realnam.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="161" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Realnam.JPG/280px-Realnam.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/55/Realnam.JPG 1.5x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Realnam.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Herero and Nama territories</div></div></div>
<p>By 1500 AD, most of southern Africa had established states. In northwestern <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ovambo_people" title="Ovambo people">Ovambo</a> engaged in farming and the <a href="/wiki/Herero_people" title="Herero people">Herero</a> engaged in herding. As cattle numbers increased, the Herero moved southward to central Namibia for grazing land. A related group, the <a href="/wiki/Mbanderu_people" title="Mbanderu people">Ovambanderu</a>, expanded to <a href="/wiki/Ghanzi" title="Ghanzi">Ghanzi</a> in northwestern <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nama_people" title="Nama people">Nama</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoi-speaking</a>, sheep-raising group, moved northward and came into contact with the Herero; this would set the stage for much conflict between the two groups. The expanding <a href="/wiki/Lozi_people" title="Lozi people">Lozi</a> states pushed the <a href="/wiki/Mbukushu" title="Mbukushu">Mbukushu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuhane_language" title="Kuhane language">Subiya</a>, and Yei to Botei, <a href="/wiki/Kavango_Region" title="Kavango Region">Okavango</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chobe_District" title="Chobe District">Chobe</a> in northern Botswana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa_and_Botswana">South Africa and Botswana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: South Africa and Botswana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_South_Africa" title="Early history of South Africa">Early history of South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa" title="History of South Africa">History of South Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Botswana" title="History of Botswana">History of Botswana</a></div>
<h5><span id="Sotho.E2.80.93Tswana"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sotho–Tswana">Sotho–Tswana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Sotho–Tswana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Realzulu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="161" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="109" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Realzulu.JPG/280px-Realzulu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Realzulu.JPG 1.5x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Realzulu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South African ethnic groups</div></div></div>
<p>The development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sotho%E2%80%93Tswana_peoples" title="Sotho–Tswana peoples">Sotho–Tswana</a> states based on the <a href="/wiki/Highveld" title="Highveld">highveld</a>, south of the <a href="/wiki/Limpopo_River" title="Limpopo River">Limpopo River</a>, began around 1000 CE. The chief's power rested on cattle and his connection to the ancestor. This can be seen in the <a href="/wiki/Toutswemogala_Hill" title="Toutswemogala Hill">Toutswemogala Hill</a> settlements with stone foundations and stone walls, north of the highveld and south of the <a href="/wiki/Vaal_River" title="Vaal River">Vaal River</a>. Northwest of the Vaal River developed early <a href="/wiki/Tswana_people" title="Tswana people">Tswana</a> states centered on towns of thousands of people. When disagreements or rivalry arose, different groups moved to form their own states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Nguni_peoples">Nguni peoples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Nguni peoples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Southeast of the <a href="/wiki/Drakensberg" title="Drakensberg">Drakensberg</a> mountains lived <a href="/wiki/Nguni_languages" title="Nguni languages">Nguni-speaking</a> peoples (<a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swazi_people" title="Swazi people">Swazi</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Ndebele_people" title="South Ndebele people">Ndebele</a>). They too engaged in state building, with new states developing from rivalry, disagreements, and population pressure causing movement into new regions. This 19th-century process of warfare, state building and migration later became known as the Mfecane (Nguni) or Difaqane (Sotho). Its major catalyst was the consolidation of the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Kingdom" title="Zulu Kingdom">Zulu Kingdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> They were metalworkers, cultivators of millet, and cattle herders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Khoisan_and_Boers">Khoisan and Boers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Khoisan and Boers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SouthAfrica1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2884" data-file-width="3336" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SouthAfrica1885.jpg/280px-SouthAfrica1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SouthAfrica1885.jpg/420px-SouthAfrica1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/SouthAfrica1885.jpg/560px-SouthAfrica1885.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SouthAfrica1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Political map of Southern Africa in 1885</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Khoisan" title="Khoisan">Khoisan</a> lived in the southwestern <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape Province</a>, where winter rainfall is plentiful. Earlier Khoisan populations were absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu peoples</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sotho_people" title="Sotho people">Sotho</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nguni_people" title="Nguni people">Nguni</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">Bantu expansion</a> stopped at the region with winter rainfall. Some <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a> have incorporated the <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">click consonant</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Khoisan_languages" title="Khoisan languages">Khoisan languages</a>. The Khoisan traded with their Bantu neighbors, providing cattle, sheep, and hunted items. In return, their Bantu speaking neighbors traded copper, iron, and tobacco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155_217-2"><a href="#cite_note-Shillington_2005,_pp._153–155-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 16th century, the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> established a replenishing station at <a href="/wiki/Table_Bay" title="Table Bay">Table Bay</a> for restocking water and purchasing meat from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" title="Khoikhoi">Khoikhoi</a>. The Khoikhoi received copper, iron, tobacco, and beads in exchange. In order to control the price of meat and stock and make service more consistent, the Dutch established a permanent settlement at Table Bay in 1652. They grew fresh fruit and vegetables and established a hospital for sick sailors. To increase produce, the Dutch decided to increase the number of farms at Table Bay by encouraging freeburgher <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">boers</a></i> (farmers) on lands worked initially by slaves from West Africa. The land was taken from Khoikhoi grazing land, triggering the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoikhoi-Dutch_Wars" title="Khoikhoi-Dutch Wars">Khoikhoi-Dutch war</a> in 1659. No victors emerged, but the Dutch assumed a "<a href="/wiki/Right_of_conquest" title="Right of conquest">right of conquest</a>" by which they claimed all of the cape. In a series of wars pitting the Khoikhoi against each other, the Boers assumed all Khoikhoi land and claimed all their cattle. The second Khoikoi-Dutch war (1673–1677) was a cattle raid. The Khoikhoi also died in thousands from European diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 18th century, the cape colony had grown, with slaves coming from <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. The settlement also started to expand northward, but Khoikhoi resistance, raids, and <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla warfare</a> slowed the expansion during the 18th century. Boers who started to practice pastoralism were known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trekboer" title="Trekboer">trekboers</a></i>. A common source of <i>trekboer</i> labor was orphan children who were captured during raids and whose parents had been killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Africa">Southeast Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Southeast Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa#History" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa § History</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_2">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to the theory of <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">recent African origin of modern humans</a>, the mainstream position held within the scientific community, all humans originate from either <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> During the first millennium CE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nilotic" title="Nilotic">Nilotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a>-speaking peoples <a href="/wiki/Bantu_expansion" title="Bantu expansion">moved into the region</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Swahili_coast">Swahili coast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Swahili coast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Swahili_coast#History" title="Swahili coast">Swahili coast § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg/180px-Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg/270px-Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg/360px-Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swahili_door_Zanzibar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional Zanzibari-style <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> coast door in <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the Bantu Migration, on the coastal section of Southeast Africa, a mixed Bantu community developed through contact with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian</a> traders, leading to the development of the mixed Arab, Persian and African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili City States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Swahili_culture" title="Swahili culture">Swahili culture</a> that emerged from these exchanges evinces many Arab and Islamic influences not seen in traditional Bantu culture, as do the many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Arab" title="Afro-Arab">Afro-Arab</a> members of the Bantu <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a>. With its original speech community centered on the coastal parts of <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> (particularly <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>—a seaboard referred to as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swahili_Coast" title="Swahili Coast">Swahili Coast</a>—the Bantu <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili language</a> contains many <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> language <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loan-words</a> as a consequence of these interactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu inhabitants</a> of the Southeast coast of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> encountered by these later Arab and Persian settlers have been variously identified with the trading settlements of <a href="/wiki/Rhapta" title="Rhapta">Rhapta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azania" title="Azania">Azania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Menouthias" title="Menouthias">Menouthias</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> referenced in early Greek and Chinese writings from 50 AD to 500 AD,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> ultimately giving rise to the name for <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> These early writings perhaps document the first wave of Bantu settlers to reach Southeast Africa during their migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a> could be found as far north as northern <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and as far south as the <a href="/wiki/Ruvuma_River" title="Ruvuma River">Ruvuma River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>. Arab geographers referred to the Swahili coast as the land of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zanj" title="Zanj">zanj</a></i> (blacks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although once believed to be the descendants of Persian colonists, the ancient Swahili are now recognized by most historians, historical linguists, and archaeologists as a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> people who had sustained important interactions with Muslim merchants, beginning in the late 7th and early 8th centuries AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/220px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/330px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/440px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arab slave traders and their captives along the <a href="/wiki/Ruvuma_River" title="Ruvuma River">Ruvuma River</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> along the Swahili coast.</div></div></div>
<p>Medieval Swahili kingdoms are known to have had island trade ports, described by Greek historians as "<a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolises</a>", and to have established regular trade routes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Islamic world</a> and Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> Ports such as <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zanzibar" title="Zanzibar">Zanzibar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kilwa_Kisiwani" title="Kilwa Kisiwani">Kilwa</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> were known to Chinese sailors under <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> and medieval Islamic geographers such as the Berber traveller <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Abu Abdullah ibn Battuta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> The main Swahili exports were ivory, slaves, and gold. They traded with Arabia, India, Persia, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Portuguese arrived in 1498. On a mission to economically control and <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">Christianize</a> the Swahili coast, the Portuguese attacked Kilwa first in 1505 and other cities later. Because of Swahili resistance, the Portuguese attempt at establishing commercial control was never successful. By the late 17th century, Portuguese authority on the Swahili coast began to diminish. With the help of <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Omani</a> Arabs, by 1729 the Portuguese presence had been removed. The Swahili coast eventually became part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Oman" title="Sultanate of Oman">Sultanate of Oman</a>. Trade recovered, but it did not regain the levels of the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPage2001263–264_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPage2001263–264-243">[243]</a></sup>
</p>
