<div style="position:absolute; font-size:85%; left:42px; top:80px"><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benin_city" title="Benin city">Idu</a></b></div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Niger_Delta" title="Niger Delta">Niger Delta</a> comprised numerous <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> with numerous forms of government. These city-states were protected by the waterways and thick vegetation of the delta. The region was transformed by trade in the 17th century. The delta's city-states were comparable to those of the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili people</a> in East Africa. Some, like <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bonny" title="Kingdom of Bonny">Bonny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalabari_Kingdom" title="Kalabari Kingdom">Kalabari</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Warri" title="Kingdom of Warri">Warri</a>, had kings. Others, like <a href="/wiki/Brass,_Nigeria" title="Brass, Nigeria">Brass</a>, were republics with small senates, and those at <a href="/wiki/Cross_River_State" title="Cross River State">Cross River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calabar" title="Calabar">Old Calabar</a> were ruled by merchants of the <i>ekpe</i> society. The <i>ekpe</i> society regulated trade and made rules for members known as house systems. Some of these houses, like the Pepples of Bonny, were well known in the Americas and Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[284]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> lived east of the delta (but with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anioma" title="Anioma">Anioma</a> on the west of the Niger River). The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Nri" title="Kingdom of Nri">Kingdom of Nri</a> rose in the 9th century, with the <i>Eze</i> Nri being its leader. It was a political entity composed of villages, and each village was autonomous and independent with its own territory and name, each recognized by its neighbors. Villages were democratic with all males and sometimes females a part of the decision-making process. Graves at <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu" title="Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a> (800 AD) contained brass artifacts of local manufacture and glass beads from Egypt or India, indicative of extraregional trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=65" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Africa_2">Southern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=66" title="Edit section: Southern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa#History" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa § History</a></div>
<p>By the 1850s, British and German missionaries and traders had penetrated present-day <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Herero_people" title="Herero people">Herero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nama_people" title="Nama people">Nama</a> peoples competed for guns and ammunition, providing cattle, ivory, and ostrich feathers. The Germans were more firmly established than the British in the region. By 1884, the <a href="/wiki/German_colonial_empire" title="German colonial empire">Germans</a> declared the coastal region from the <a href="/wiki/Orange_River" title="Orange River">Orange River</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Cunene_River" title="Cunene River">Kunene River</a> a German protectorate, part of <a href="/wiki/German_South_West_Africa" title="German South West Africa">German South West Africa</a>. They pursued an aggressive policy of land expansion for white settlements. They exploited rivalry between the Nama and Herero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Herero entered into an alliance with the Germans, thinking they could get an upper hand on the Nama. The Germans set up a garrison at the Herero capital and started allocating Herero land for white settlements, including the best grazing land in the central plateau, and made tax and labor demands. The Herero and <a href="/wiki/Mbanderu_people" title="Mbanderu people">Ovambanderu</a> rebelled, but the rebellion was crushed and leaders were executed. Between 1896 and 1897, <a href="/wiki/Rinderpest" title="Rinderpest">rinderpest</a> impaired the Herero and Nama economy and slowed white expansion. The Germans continued the policy of making Namibia a white settlement by seizing land and cattle, and even trying to export Herero labor to South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-288">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1904, the Herero rebelled again. German General <a href="/wiki/Lothar_von_Trotha" title="Lothar von Trotha">Lothar von Trotha</a> implemented an <a href="/wiki/Herero_and_Namaqua_genocide" title="Herero and Namaqua genocide">extermination policy</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterberg" title="Battle of Waterberg">Battle of Waterberg</a>, which drove the Herero west of the <a href="/wiki/Kalahari_Desert" title="Kalahari Desert">Kalahari Desert</a>. At the end of 1905, only 16,000 Herero were alive, out of a previous population of 80,000. Nama resistance was crushed in 1907. All Nama and Herero cattle and land were confiscated from the very diminished population, with remaining Nama and Herero assuming a subordinate position. Labor had to be imported from among the Ovambo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_288-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-288">[288]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nguniland">Nguniland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=67" title="Edit section: Nguniland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nguni_people#History" title="Nguni people">Nguni people § History</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dance_of_Landeens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="758" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Dance_of_Landeens.jpg/220px-Dance_of_Landeens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Dance_of_Landeens.jpg/330px-Dance_of_Landeens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Dance_of_Landeens.jpg/440px-Dance_of_Landeens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dance_of_Landeens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zulu warriors</div></div></div>
<p>A moment of great disorder in southern Africa was the <i><a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a></i>, "the crushing." It was started by the northern Nguni kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Mthethwa_Paramountcy" title="Mthethwa Paramountcy">Mthethwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ndwandwe" title="Ndwandwe">Ndwandwe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a> over scarce resource and famine. When <a href="/wiki/Dingiswayo" title="Dingiswayo">Dingiswayo</a> of Mthethwa died, <a href="/wiki/Shaka" title="Shaka">Shaka</a> of the Zulu people took over. He established the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Kingdom" title="Zulu Kingdom">Zulu Kingdom</a>, asserting authority over the Ndwandwe and pushing the Swazi north. The scattering Ndwandwe and Swazi caused the Mfecane to spread. During the 1820s, Shaka expanded the empire all along the Drakensberg foothills, with tribute being paid as far south as the <a href="/wiki/Tugela_River" title="Tugela River">Tugela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umzimkulu_River" title="Umzimkulu River">Umzimkulu</a> rivers. He replaced the chiefs of conquered polities with <i>indunas</i>, responsible to him. He introduced a centralized, dedicated, and disciplined military force not seen in the region, with a new weapon in the short stabbing-spear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1828, Shaka was assassinated by his half brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dingane_kaSenzangakhona" title="Dingane kaSenzangakhona">Dingane</a>, who lacked the military genius and leadership skills of Shaka. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voortrekkers" title="Voortrekkers">Voortrekkers</a> tried to occupy Zulu land in 1838. In the early months they were defeated, but the survivors regrouped at the <a href="/wiki/Blood_River" title="Blood River">Ncome River</a> and soundly defeated the Zulu. However, the Voortrekkers dared not settle Zulu land. Dingane was killed in 1840 during a civil war. His brother <a href="/wiki/Mpande" title="Mpande">Mpande</a> took over and strengthened Zulu territories to the north. In 1879 <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">the Zulu Kingdom was invaded</a> by Britain in a quest to control all of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. The Zulu Kingdom was victorious at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Isandlwana" title="Battle of Isandlwana">Battle of Isandlwana</a> but was defeated at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ulundi" title="Battle of Ulundi">Battle of Ulundi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991274–275_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991274–275-291">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the major states to emerge from the <a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Sotho Kingdom</a> founded at <a href="/wiki/Thaba_Bosiu" title="Thaba Bosiu">Thaba Bosiu</a> by <a href="/wiki/Moshoeshoe_I" title="Moshoeshoe I">Moshoeshoe I</a> around 1821 to 1822. It was a <a href="/wiki/Confederation" title="Confederation">confederation</a> of different polities that accepted the absolute authority of Moshoeshoe. During the 1830s, the kingdom invited missionaries as a strategic means of acquiring guns and horses from the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Province" title="Cape Province">Cape</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a> slowly diminished the kingdom but never completely defeated it. In 1868, Moshoeshoe asked that the Sotho Kingdom be annexed by Britain, to save the remnant. It became the British protectorate of <a href="/wiki/Basutoland" title="Basutoland">Basutoland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sotho-Tswana">Sotho-Tswana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=68" title="Edit section: Sotho-Tswana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sotho_people#History" title="Sotho people">Sotho people § History</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tswana_people#History" title="Tswana people">Tswana people § History</a></div>
<p>The arrival of the ancestors of the Tswana-speakers who came to control the region (from the <a href="/wiki/Vaal_River" title="Vaal River">Vaal River</a> to Botswana) has yet to be dated precisely although AD 600 seems to be a consensus estimate. This massive cattle-raising complex prospered until 1300 AD or so. All these various peoples were connected to trade routes that ran via the Limpopo River to the Indian Ocean, and trade goods from Asia such as beads made their way to Botswana most likely in exchange for ivory, gold, and rhinoceros horn. The first written records relating to modern-day Botswana appear in 1824. What these records show is that the Bangwaketse had become the predominant power in the region. Under the rule of Makaba II, the Bangwaketse kept vast herds of cattle in well-protected desert areas, and used their military prowess to raid their neighbours. Other chiefdoms in the area, by this time, had capitals of 10,000 or so and were fairly prosperous. This equilibrium came to end during the Mfecane period, 1823–1843, when a succession of invading peoples from South Africa entered the country. Although the Bangwaketse were able to defeat the invading Bakololo in 1826, over time all the major chiefdoms in Botswana were attacked, weakened, and impoverished. The Bakololo and Amandebele raided repeatedly, and took large numbers of cattle, women, and children from the Batswana—most of whom were driven into the desert or sanctuary areas such as hilltops and caves. Only after 1843, when the Amandebele moved into western Zimbabwe, did this threat subside.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> During the 1840s and 1850s trade with Cape Colony-based merchants opened up and enabled the Batswana chiefdoms to rebuild. The Bakwena, Bangwaketse, Bangwato and Batawana cooperated to control the lucrative ivory trade, and then used the proceeds to import horses and guns, which in turn enabled them to establish control over what is now Botswana. This process was largely complete by 1880, and thus the Bushmen, the Bakalanga, the Bakgalagadi, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tswapong_people" title="Tswapong people">Batswapong</a> and other current minorities were subjugated by the Batswana. Following the Great Trek, Afrikaners from the Cape Colony established themselves on the borders of Botswana in the Transvaal. In 1852 a coalition of Tswana chiefdoms led by Sechele I resisted Afrikaner incursions, and after about eight years of intermittent tensions and hostilities, eventually came to a peace agreement in Potchefstroom in 1860. From that point on, the modern-day border between South Africa and Botswana was agreed on, and the Afrikaners and Batswana traded and worked together peacefully.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[295]</a></sup> In the 1820s, refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_people" title="Zulu people">Zulu</a> expansion under <a href="/wiki/Shaka" title="Shaka">Shaka</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[296]</a></sup> came into contact with the Basotho people residing on the highveld. In 1823, those pressures caused one group of Basotho, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makololo" title="Makololo">Kololo</a>, to migrate north, past the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okavango_Swamp" title="Okavango Swamp">Okavango Swamp</a> and across the <a href="/wiki/Zambezi" title="Zambezi">Zambezi</a> into Barotseland, now part of <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-297"><a href="#cite_note-297">[297]</a></sup> In 1845, the Kololo conquered Barotseland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[298]</a></sup>
</p><p>At about the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Boers" title="Boers">Boers</a> began to encroach upon Basotho territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_2009_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ross_2009-299">[299]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Colony" title="Cape Colony">Cape Colony</a> had been ceded to Britain at the conclusion of the Napoleonic Wars, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voortrekker" title="Voortrekker">voortrekkers</a></i> ("pioneers") were farmers who opted to leave the former Dutch colony and moved inland where they eventually established independent polities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_2009_299-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ross_2009-299">[299]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_2001_300-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_2001-300">[300]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the time of these developments, <a href="/wiki/Moshoeshoe_I" title="Moshoeshoe I">Moshoeshoe I</a> gained control of the Basotho kingdoms of the southern Highveld.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_2001_300-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson_2001-300">[300]</a></sup> Universally praised as a skilled diplomat and strategist, he was able to wield the disparate refugee groups escaping the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Difaqane" title="Difaqane">Difaqane</a> into a cohesive nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-301">[301]</a></sup> His inspired leadership helped his small nation to survive the dangers and pitfalls (the Zulu hegemony, the inward expansion of the voortrekkers and the designs of imperial Britain) that destroyed other indigenous South African kingdoms during the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[302]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1822, Moshoeshoe established his capital at <a href="/wiki/Butha-Buthe" title="Butha-Buthe">Butha-Buthe</a>, an easily defensible mountain in the northern Drakensberg mountains, laying the foundations of the eventual Kingdom of Lesotho.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_301-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-301">[301]</a></sup> His capital was later moved to Thaba Bosiu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_301-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-301">[301]</a></sup>
</p><p>To deal with the encroaching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voortrekker" title="Voortrekker">voortrekker</a> groups, Moshoeshoe encouraged French missionary activity in his kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-303">[303]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missionaries" title="Missionaries">Missionaries</a> sent by the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Evangelical_Missionary_Society" title="Paris Evangelical Missionary Society">Paris Evangelical Missionary Society</a> provided the King with foreign affairs counsel and helped to facilitate the purchase of modern weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-303">[303]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from acting as state ministers, missionaries (primarily Casalis and Arbousset) played a vital role in delineating Sesotho <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">orthography</a> and printing Sesotho language materials between 1837 and 1855.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[304]</a></sup> The first Sesotho translation of the Bible appeared in 1878.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-305"><a href="#cite_note-305">[305]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1868, after losing the western lowlands to the Boers during the <a href="/wiki/Free_State%E2%80%93Basotho_Wars" title="Free State–Basotho Wars">Free State–Basotho Wars</a>; Moshoeshoe successfully appealed to <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> to proclaim <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a> (then known as Basutoland) a <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> of Britain and the British administration was placed in <a href="/wiki/Maseru" title="Maseru">Maseru</a>, the site of Lesotho's current capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_2009_299-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ross_2009-299">[299]</a></sup> Local chieftains retained power over internal affairs while Britain was responsible for foreign affairs and the defence of the protectorate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[306]</a></sup> In 1869, the British sponsored a process by which the borders of Basutoland were finally demarcated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_2009_299-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ross_2009-299">[299]</a></sup> While many clans had territory within Basutoland, large numbers of Sesotho speakers resided in areas allocated to the <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a>, the sovereign voortrekker republic that bordered the Basotho kingdom.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Voortrekkers">Voortrekkers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=69" title="Edit section: Voortrekkers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1087" data-file-width="1376" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/220px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/330px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg/440px-Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charles_Bell_-_Zoeloe-aanval_op_%27n_Boerelaer_-_1838.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Depiction of a <a href="/wiki/Weenen_massacre" title="Weenen massacre">Zulu attack</a> on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boer</a> camp in February 1838</div></div></div>
<p>By the 19th century, most Khoikhoi territory was under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boer</a> control. The Khoikhoi had lost economic and political independence and had been absorbed into Boer society. The Boers spoke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrikaans_language" title="Afrikaans language">Afrikaans</a>, a language or dialect derived from Dutch, and no longer called themselves Boers but <a href="/wiki/Afrikaners" title="Afrikaners">Afrikaners</a>. Some Khoikhoi were used as <a href="/wiki/Commando" title="Commando">commandos</a> in raids against other Khoikhoi and later Xhosa. A mixed Khoi, slave, and European population called the <a href="/wiki/Cape_Coloureds" title="Cape Coloureds">Cape Coloureds</a>, who were outcasts within colonial society, also arose. Khoikhoi who lived far on the frontier included the Kora, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oorlams_creole" title="Oorlams creole">Oorlams</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Griqua_people" title="Griqua people">Griqua</a>. In 1795, the British took over the cape colony from the Dutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[307]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1830s, Boers embarked on a journey of expansion, east of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fish_River" title="Great Fish River">Great Fish River</a> into the Zuurveld. They were referred to as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voortrekkers" title="Voortrekkers">Voortrekkers</a></i>. They founded republics of the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a>, mostly in areas of sparse population that had been diminished by the <i><a href="/wiki/Mfecane" title="Mfecane">Mfecane/Difaqane</a></i>. Unlike the Khoisan, the Bantu states were not conquered by the Afrikaners, because of population density and greater unity. Additionally, they began to arm themselves with guns acquired through trade at the cape. In some cases, as in the <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_Wars" title="Xhosa Wars">Xhosa/Boer Wars</a>, Boers were removed from <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_people" title="Xhosa people">Xhosa</a> lands. It required a dedicated imperial military force to subdue the Bantu-speaking states. In 1901, the Boer republics were defeated by Britain in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>. The defeat however consummated many Afrikaners' ambition: South Africa would be under white rule. The British placed all power—legislative, executive, administrative—in English and Afrikaner hands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991274–275_291-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavidson1991274–275-291">[291]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="European_trade.2C_exploration_and_conquest"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="European_trade,_exploration_and_conquest">European trade, exploration and conquest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=70" title="Edit section: European trade, exploration and conquest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_slave_trade" title="African slave trade">African slave trade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1225" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg/220px-Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg/330px-Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg/440px-Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maxim_machine_gun_Megapixie.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1895 .303 tripod mounted Maxim machine gun</div></div></div>
<p>Between 1878 and 1898, European states partitioned and conquered most of Africa. For 400 years, European nations had mainly limited their involvement to trading stations on the African coast. Few dared venture inland from the coast; those that did, like the Portuguese, often met defeats and had to retreat to the coast. Several technological innovations helped to overcome this 400-year pattern. One was the development of <a href="/wiki/Repeating_rifle" title="Repeating rifle">repeating rifles</a>, which were easier and quicker to load than <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">muskets</a>. <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">Artillery</a> was being used increasingly. In 1885, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hiram_S._Maxim" title="Hiram S. Maxim">Hiram S. Maxim</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Maxim_gun" title="Maxim gun">maxim gun</a>, the model of the modern-day <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine gun</a>. European states kept these weapons largely among themselves by refusing to sell these weapons to African leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[309]</a></sup>
</p><p>African germs took numerous European lives and deterred permanent settlements. Diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yaws" title="Yaws">yaws</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Leprosy" title="Leprosy">leprosy</a> made Africa a very inhospitable place for Europeans. The deadliest disease was <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, endemic throughout <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Africa" title="Tropical Africa">Tropical Africa</a>. In 1854, the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a> and other medical innovations helped to make conquest and colonization in Africa possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[310]</a></sup>
</p><p>Strong motives for conquest of Africa were at play. Raw materials were needed for European factories. Europe in the early part of the 19th century was undergoing its <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. Nationalist rivalries and prestige were at play. Acquiring African colonies would show rivals that a nation was powerful and significant. These factors culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[311]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2340" data-file-width="3144" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg/220px-David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg/330px-David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg/440px-David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_Livingstone_attacked_by_a_lion_in_Africa._Lithograph._Wellcome_V0018847.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>, early European explorer of the interior of Africa, is attacked by a <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lion</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_journey_to_Ashango-Land,_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_(1874)_(14594947249).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1984" data-file-width="3224" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg/220px-A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg/330px-A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg/440px-A_journey_to_Ashango-Land%2C_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_%281874%29_%2814594947249%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_journey_to_Ashango-Land,_and_further_penetration_into_equatorial_Africa_(1874)_(14594947249).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French explorer <a href="/wiki/Paul_Du_Chaillu" title="Paul Du Chaillu">Paul Du Chaillu</a> confirmed the existence of <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_peoples" title="Pygmy peoples">Pygmy peoples</a> of central Africa</div></div></div>
<p>Knowledge of Africa increased. Numerous European explorers began to explore the continent. <a href="/wiki/Mungo_Park_(explorer)" title="Mungo Park (explorer)">Mungo Park</a> traversed the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>. <a href="/wiki/James_Bruce" title="James Bruce">James Bruce</a> travelled through <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and located the source of the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Nile" title="Blue Nile">Blue Nile</a>. <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a> was the first European at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_White_Baker" title="Samuel White Baker">Samuel White Baker</a> explored the Upper Nile. <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a> located a source of the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria" title="Lake Victoria">Lake Victoria</a>. Other significant European explorers included <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Barth" title="Heinrich Barth">Heinrich Barth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a> (coiner of the term "<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Dark_Continent" title="wikt:Dark Continent">Dark Continent</a>" for Africa in an 1878 book), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ant%C3%B3nio_Silva_Porto" title="António Silva Porto">Silva Porto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_de_Serpa_Pinto" title="Alexandre de Serpa Pinto">Alexandre de Serpa Pinto</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rene_Caille" title="Rene Caille">Rene Caille</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Gerhard_Rohlfs" title="Friedrich Gerhard Rohlfs">Friedrich Gerhard Rohlfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Nachtigal" title="Gustav Nachtigal">Gustav Nachtigal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Schweinfurth" title="George Schweinfurth">George Schweinfurth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Thomson_(explorer)" title="Joseph Thomson (explorer)">Joseph Thomson</a>. The most famous of the explorers was <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a>, who explored southern Africa and traversed the continent from the Atlantic at <a href="/wiki/Luanda" title="Luanda">Luanda</a> to the Indian Ocean at <a href="/wiki/Quelimane" title="Quelimane">Quelimane</a>. European explorers made use of African guides and servants, and established long-distance trading routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[312]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_2007_pp._254–257_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_2007_pp._254–257-313">[313]</a></sup>
</p><p>Missionaries attempting to spread <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> also increased European knowledge of Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_2007_pp._254–257_313-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_2007_pp._254–257-313">[313]</a></sup> Between 1884 and 1885, European nations met at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference_(1884)" title="Berlin Conference (1884)">Berlin West Africa Conference</a> to discuss the partitioning of Africa. It was agreed that European claims to parts of Africa would only be recognised if Europeans provided effective occupation. In a series of treaties in 1890–1891, colonial boundaries were completely drawn. All of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> was claimed by European powers, except for Ethiopia (Abyssinia) and <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[314]</a></sup>
</p><p>The European powers set up a variety of different administrations in Africa, reflecting different ambitions and degrees of power. In some areas, such as parts of <a href="/wiki/British_West_Africa" title="British West Africa">British West Africa</a>, colonial control was tenuous and intended for simple economic extraction, strategic power, or as part of a long-term development plan. In other areas, Europeans were encouraged to settle, creating settler states in which a European minority dominated. Settlers only came to a few colonies in sufficient numbers to have a strong impact. British settler colonies included <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_Africa" title="British East Africa">British East Africa</a> (now Kenya), <a href="/wiki/Northern_Rhodesia" title="Northern Rhodesia">Northern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Rhodesia" title="Southern Rhodesia">Southern Rhodesia</a>, (<a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, respectively), and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, which already had a significant population of European settlers, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boer" title="Boer">Boers</a>. France planned to settle <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">Algeria</a> and eventually incorporate it into the French state on an equal basis with the European provinces. Algeria's proximity across the Mediterranean allowed plans of this scale.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In most areas colonial administrations did not have the manpower or resources to fully administer the territory and had to rely on local power structures to help them. Various factions and groups within the societies exploited this European requirement for their own purposes, attempting to gain positions of power within their own communities by cooperating with Europeans. One aspect of this struggle included what <a href="/wiki/Terence_Ranger" title="Terence Ranger">Terence Ranger</a> has termed the "invention of tradition." In order to legitimize their own claims to power in the eyes of both the colonial administrators and their own people, native elites would essentially manufacture "traditional" claims to power, or ceremonies. As a result, many societies were thrown into disarray by the new order.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>, an early but secondary focus for most <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonial</a> <a href="/wiki/Regime" title="Regime">regimes</a> was the suppression of slavery and the slave trade. By the end of the colonial period they were mostly successful in this aim, though slavery is still very active in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[315]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France_versus_Britain:_the_Fashoda_crisis_of_1898">France versus Britain: the Fashoda crisis of 1898</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=71" title="Edit section: France versus Britain: the Fashoda crisis of 1898">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2740" data-file-width="3384" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg/350px-Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg/525px-Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg/700px-Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fashoda_Incident_map_-_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Central and east Africa, 1898, during the <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">Fashoda Incident</a></div></div></div>
<p>As a part of the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>, France had the establishment of a continuous west–east axis of the continent as an objective, in contrast with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_to_Cairo_Red_Line" title="Cape to Cairo Red Line">the British north–south axis</a>. Tensions between Britain and France reached tinder stage in Africa. At several points war was possible, but never happened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[316]</a></sup> The most serious episode was the <a href="/wiki/Fashoda_Incident" title="Fashoda Incident">Fashoda Incident</a> of 1898. French troops tried to claim an area in the Southern Sudan, and a much more powerful British force purporting to be acting in the interests of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khedive_of_Egypt" title="Khedive of Egypt">Khedive of Egypt</a> arrived to confront them. Under heavy pressure the French withdrew securing British control over the area. The status quo was recognised by an agreement between the two states acknowledging British control over Egypt, while France became the dominant power in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, but France suffered a humiliating defeat overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[317]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[318]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_colonial_territories">European colonial territories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=72" title="Edit section: European colonial territories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
