<p><b>Independent states</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, founded by the <a href="/wiki/American_Colonization_Society" title="American Colonization Society">American Colonization Society</a> of the United States in 1821; declared independence in 1847</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Empire" title="Ethiopian Empire">Ethiopian Empire</a> (Abyssinia) had its borders re-drawn with <a href="/wiki/Italian_Eritrea" title="Italian Eritrea">Italian Eritrea</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_Somaliland" title="French Somaliland">French Somaliland</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>), briefly occupied by Italy from 1936 to 1941 during the <a href="/wiki/Abyssinia_Crisis" title="Abyssinia Crisis">Abyssinia Crisis</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, independent under <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmad" title="Muhammad Ahmad">Mahdi</a> rule between 1885 and 1899. It was then under British rule from 1899 to 1956.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[319]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="20th_century">20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=73" title="Edit section: 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In the 1880s the European powers had divided up almost all of Africa (only Ethiopia and Liberia were independent). They ruled until after World War II when forces of nationalism grew much stronger. In the 1950s and 1960s the colonial holdings became independent states. The process was usually peaceful but there were several long bitter bloody civil wars, as in Algeria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-320">[320]</a></sup> Kenya<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[321]</a></sup> and elsewhere. Across Africa the powerful new force of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a> drew upon the organizational skills that natives learned in the British and French and other armies in the world wars. It led to organizations that were not controlled by or endorsed by either the colonial powers not the traditional local power structures that were collaborating with the colonial powers. Nationalistic organizations began to challenge both the traditional and the new colonial structures and finally displaced them. Leaders of nationalist movements took control when the European authorities exited; many ruled for decades or until they died off. These structures included political, educational, religious, and other social organizations. In recent decades, many African countries have undergone the triumph and defeat of nationalistic fervor, changing in the process the loci of the centralizing state power and patrimonial state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[322]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[323]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[324]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1916-Africa-political-map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2298" data-file-width="3244" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/1916-Africa-political-map.jpg/220px-1916-Africa-political-map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/1916-Africa-political-map.jpg/330px-1916-Africa-political-map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/1916-Africa-political-map.jpg/440px-1916-Africa-political-map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1916-Africa-political-map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1916 political map of Africa</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_I">World War I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=74" title="Edit section: World War I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1737" data-file-width="2753" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg/220px-Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg/330px-Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg/440px-Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Der_Durchbruch_der_Schutztruppe_Deutsch-Ostafrika_%C3%BCber_den_Rowuma_MItte_November_1917._Darstellung_von_Carl_Arriens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ngomano" title="Battle of Ngomano">Battle of Ngomano</a> in November 1917</div></div></div>
<p>With the vast majority of the continent under the colonial control of European governments, the World Wars were significant events in the geopolitical history of Africa. Africa was a theater of war and saw fighting in both wars. More important in most regions, the <a href="/wiki/Total_war" title="Total war">total war</a> footing of colonial powers impacted the governance of African colonies, through resource allocation, conscription, and taxation. In <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> there were several campaigns in Africa, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Togoland_Campaign" title="Togoland Campaign">Togoland Campaign</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamerun_Campaign" title="Kamerun Campaign">Kamerun Campaign</a>, the <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa_campaign" title="South West Africa campaign">South West Africa campaign</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_I)" title="East African Campaign (World War I)">East African campaign</a>. In each, Allied forces, primarily British, but also French, Belgian, South African, and Portuguese, sought to force the Germans out of their African colonies. In each, German forces were badly outnumbered and, due to Allied naval superiority, were cut off from reinforcement or resupply. The Allies eventually conquered all German colonies; German forces in East Africa managed to avoid surrender throughout the war, though they could not hold any territory after 1917. After World War I, <a href="/wiki/List_of_former_German_colonies" title="List of former German colonies">former German colonies</a> in Africa were taken over by France, Belgium, and the British Empire.
</p><p>After World War I, colonial powers continued to consolidate their control over their African territories. In some areas, particularly in Southern and East Africa, large settler populations were successful in pressing for additional devolution of administration, so-called "home rule" by the white settlers. In many cases, settler regimes were harsher on African populations, tending to see them more as a threat to political power, as opposed to colonial regimes which had generally endeavored to co-opt local populations into economic production. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> strongly affected Africa's non-subsistence economy, much of which was based on commodity production for Western markets. As demand increased in the late 1930s, Africa's economy rebounded as well.
</p><p>Africa was the site of one of the first instances of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fascist" title="Fascist">fascist</a> territorial expansions in the 1930s. Italy had attempted to conquer Ethiopia in the 1890s but had been rebuffed in the <a href="/wiki/First_Italo-Ethiopian_War" title="First Italo-Ethiopian War">First Italo-Ethiopian War</a>. Ethiopia lay between two Italian colonies, <a href="/wiki/Italian_Somaliland" title="Italian Somaliland">Italian Somaliland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> and was invaded in October 1935. With an overwhelming advantage in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armor" title="Armor">armor</a> and aircraft, by May 1936, Italian forces had occupied the capital of <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a> and effectively declared victory. Ethiopia and their other colonies were consolidated into <a href="/wiki/Italian_East_Africa" title="Italian East Africa">Italian East Africa</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II:_Political">World War II: Political</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=75" title="Edit section: World War II: Political">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Africa_in_1939.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Map_of_Africa_in_1939.png/280px-Map_of_Africa_in_1939.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Map_of_Africa_in_1939.png 1.5x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Africa_in_1939.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Areas controlled by European powers in 1939. British (red) and Belgian (Orange) colonies fought with the Allies. Italian (green) with the Axis. French colonies (dark blue) fought alongside the Allies until the Fall of France in June 1940. Vichy was in control until the Free French prevailed in late 1942. Portuguese (brown) and Spanish (teal) colonies remained neutral.</div></div></div>
<p>Africa was a large continent whose geography gave it strategic importance during the war. North Africa was the scene of major British and American campaigns against Italy and Germany; East Africa was the scene of a major British campaign against Italy. The vast geography provided major transportation routes linking the United States to the Middle East and Mediterranean regions. The sea route around South Africa was heavily used even though it added 40 days to voyages that had to avoid the dangerous Suez region. Lend Lease supplies to Russia often came this way. Internally, long-distance road and railroad connections facilitated the British war effort. The <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_Africa" title="Union of South Africa">Union of South Africa</a> had dominion status and was largely self-governing, the other British possessions were ruled by the colonial office, usually with close ties to local chiefs and kings. Italian holdings were the target of successful British military campaigns. The Belgian Congo, and two other Belgian colonies, were major exporters. In terms of numbers and wealth, the British -controlled the richest portions of Africa, and made extensive use not only of the geography, but the manpower, and the natural resources. Civilian colonial officials made a special effort to upgrade the African infrastructure, promote agriculture, integrate colonial Africa with the world economy, and recruit over a half million soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[326]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the war, Britain had made few plans for the utilization of Africa, but it quickly set up command structures. The Army set up the West Africa Command, which recruited 200,000 soldiers. The East Africa Command was created in September 1941 to support the overstretched Middle East Command. It provided the largest number of men, over 320,000, chiefly from Kenya, Tanganyika, and Uganda. The Southern Command was the domain of South Africa. The Royal Navy set up the South Atlantic Command based in Sierra Leone, that became one of the main convoy assembly points. The RAF Coastal Command had major submarine-hunting operations based in West Africa, while a smaller RAF command Dealt with submarines in the Indian Ocean. Ferrying aircraft from North America and Britain was the major mission of the Western Desert Air Force. In addition smaller more localized commands were set up throughout the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[327]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before 1939, the military establishments were very small throughout British Africa, and largely consisted of whites, who comprised under two percent of the population outside South Africa. As soon as the war began, newly created African units were set up, primarily by the Army. The new recruits were almost always volunteers, usually provided in close cooperation with local tribal leaders. During the war, military pay scales far exceeded what civilians natives could earn, especially when food, housing and clothing allowances are included. The largest numbers were in construction units, called Pioneer units, with over 82,000 soldiers. The RAF and Navy also did some recruiting. The volunteers did some fighting, a great deal of guard duty, and construction work. 80,000 served in the Middle East. A special effort was made not to challenge white supremacy, certainly before the war, and to a large extent during the war itself. Nevertheless, the soldiers were drilled and train to European standards, given strong doses of propaganda, and learn leadership and organizational skills that proved essential to the formation of nationalistic and independence movements after 1945. There were minor episodes of discontent, but nothing serious, among the natives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[328]</a></sup> Afrikaner nationalism was a factor in South Africa, But the pro-German Afrikaner prime minister was replaced in 1939 by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Smuts" title="Jan Smuts">Jan Smuts</a>, an Afrikaner who was an enthusiastic supporter of the British Empire. His government closely cooperated with London and raised 340,000 volunteers (190,000 were white, or about one-third of the eligible white men).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[329]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="French_Africa">French Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=76" title="Edit section: French Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As early as 1857, the French established volunteer units of black soldiers in sub- Sahara Africa, termed the <i>tirailleurs senegalais.</i> They served in military operations throughout the Empire, including 171,000 soldiers in World War I and 160,000 in World War II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[330]</a></sup> About 90,000 became POWs in Germany. The veterans played a central role in the postwar independence movement in French Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[332]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa_in_World_War_II" title="French West Africa in World War II">authorities in West Africa declared allegiance to the Vichy regime</a>, as did the colony of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_Gabon" title="French Gabon">French Gabon</a> Vichy forces defeated a <a href="/wiki/Free_France" title="Free France">Free French Forces</a> invasion of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dakar" title="Battle of Dakar">two battles of Dakar</a> in July and September 1940. Gabon fell to Free France after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gabon" title="Battle of Gabon">Battle of Gabon</a> in November 1940, but West Africa remained under Vichy control until November 1942. Vichy forces tried to resist the overwhelming <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allied_landings_in_North_Africa" title="Allied landings in North Africa">Allied landings in North Africa</a> (operation <i>Torch</i>) in November 1942. Vichy Admiral <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Darlan" title="François Darlan">François Darlan</a> suddenly switched sides and the fighting ended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[333]</a></sup> The Allies gave Darlan control of North African French forces in exchange for support from both <a href="/wiki/French_North_Africa" title="French North Africa">French North Africa</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>. Vichy was now eliminated as a factor in Africa. Darlan was assassinated in December, and the two factions of Free French, led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henri_Giraud" title="Henri Giraud">Henri Giraud</a>, jockeyed for power. De Gaulle finally won out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[334]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II:_Military">World War II: Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=77" title="Edit section: World War II: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since Germany had lost its African colonies following World War I, World War II did not reach Africa until <a href="/wiki/Pact_of_Steel" title="Pact of Steel">Italy joined the war</a> on June 10, 1940, controlling Libya and Italian East Africa. With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fall_of_France" title="Fall of France">fall of France</a> on June 25, most of France's colonies in North and West Africa were controlled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vichy_government" title="Vichy government">Vichy government</a>, though much of Central Africa fell under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_French" title="Free French">Free French</a> control after some fighting between Vichy and Free French forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dakar" title="Battle of Dakar">Battle of Dakar</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gabon" title="Battle of Gabon">Battle of Gabon</a>. After the fall of France, Africa was the only active theater for ground combat until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Italian invasion of Greece">Italian invasion of Greece</a> in October. In the <a href="/wiki/Western_Desert_campaign" title="Western Desert campaign">Western Desert campaign</a> Italian forces from Libya sought to overrun Egypt, controlled by the British. Simultaneously, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/East_African_Campaign_(World_War_II)" title="East African Campaign (World War II)">East African campaign</a>, Italian East African forces overran <a href="/wiki/British_Somaliland" title="British Somaliland">British Somaliland</a> and some British outposts in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Egyptian_Sudan" title="Anglo-Egyptian Sudan">Anglo-Egyptian Sudan</a>. When Italy's efforts to conquer Egypt (including the crucial <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>) and Sudan fell short, they were unable to reestablish supply to Italian East Africa. Without the ability to reinforce or resupply and surrounded by Allied possessions, Italian East Africa was conquered by mainly British and South African forces in 1941. In North Africa, the Italians soon requested help from the Germans who sent a substantial force under General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rommel" title="Rommel">Rommel</a>. With German help, the Axis forces regained the upper hand but were unable to break through British defenses in two tries at <a href="/wiki/El_Alamein" title="El Alamein">El Alamein</a>. In late 1942, Allied forces, mainly Americans and Canadians, invaded French North Africa in <a href="/wiki/Operation_Torch" title="Operation Torch">Operation Torch</a>, where Vichy French forces initially surprised them with their resistance but were convinced to stop fighting after three days. The second front relieved pressure on the British in Egypt who began pushing west to meet up with the Torch forces, eventually pinning German and Italian forces in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, which was conquered by May 1943 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunisia_campaign" title="Tunisia campaign">Tunisia campaign</a>, ending the war in Africa. The only other significant operations occurred in the French colony of Madagascar, which was invaded by the British in May 1942 to deny its ports to the Axis (potentially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Japan" title="Imperial Japan">Japanese</a> who had reached the eastern Indian Ocean). The French garrisons in Madagascar surrendered in November 1942.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_Africa:_decolonization">Post-war Africa: decolonization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=78" title="Edit section: Post-war Africa: decolonization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historical_African_place_names" title="Historical African place names">Historical African place names</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa" title="Decolonisation of Africa">Decolonisation of Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">Neocolonialism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa_independence_dates.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Africa_independence_dates.svg/280px-Africa_independence_dates.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Africa_independence_dates.svg/420px-Africa_independence_dates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Africa_independence_dates.svg/560px-Africa_independence_dates.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa_independence_dates.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dates of independence of African countries</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decolonization_of_Africa" title="Decolonization of Africa">decolonization of Africa</a> started with <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> in 1951, although <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> were already independent. Many countries followed in the 1950s and 1960s, with a peak in 1960 with the <a href="/wiki/Year_of_Africa" title="Year of Africa">Year of Africa</a>, which saw 17 African nations declare independence, including a large part of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>. Most of the remaining countries gained independence throughout the 1960s, although some colonizers (Portugal in particular) were reluctant to relinquish sovereignty, resulting in bitter wars of independence which lasted for a decade or more. The last African countries to gain formal independence were <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a> (1974), <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> (1975) and <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> (1975) from Portugal; <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> from France in 1977; <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a> from the United Kingdom in 1980; and <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> from South Africa in 1990. <a href="/wiki/Eritrea" title="Eritrea">Eritrea</a> later split off from Ethiopia in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[335]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Africa">East Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=79" title="Edit section: East Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East_Africa#History" title="East Africa">East Africa § History</a></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mau_Mau_Uprising" title="Mau Mau Uprising">Mau Mau Uprising</a> took place in <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> from 1952 until 1956 but was put down by British and local forces. A <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">state of emergency</a> remained in place until 1960. Kenya became independent in 1963, and <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> served as <a href="/wiki/President_of_Kenya" title="President of Kenya">its first president</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[336]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early 1960s also signaled the start of major clashes between the <a href="/wiki/Hutu" title="Hutu">Hutus</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tutsi" title="Tutsi">Tutsis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>. In 1994 this culminated in the <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>, a conflict in which over 800,000 people were murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[337]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_Africa_2">North Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=80" title="Edit section: North Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_North_Africa" title="History of North Africa">History of North Africa</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Moroccan</a> nationalism developed during the 1930s; the <a href="/wiki/Istiqlal_Party" title="Istiqlal Party">Istiqlal Party</a> was formed, pushing for independence. In 1953 sultan <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_V_of_Morocco" title="Mohammed V of Morocco">Mohammed V of Morocco</a> called for independence. On March 2, 1956, Morocco became independent of France. Mohammed V became ruler of independent Morocco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[338]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> formed the <a href="/wiki/National_Liberation_Front_(Algeria)" title="National Liberation Front (Algeria)">National Liberation Front (FLN)</a> as it split from France. This resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_War" title="Algerian War">Algerian War</a>, which lasted until independence negotiations in 1962. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ahmed_Ben_Bella" title="Muhammad Ahmed Ben Bella">Muhammad Ahmed Ben Bella</a> was elected <a href="/wiki/President_of_Algeria" title="President of Algeria">President of Algeria</a>. Over a million French nationals, predominantly <a href="/wiki/Pied-Noir" title="Pied-Noir">Pied-Noirs</a>, left the country, crippling the economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_320-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-320">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1934, the "<a href="/wiki/Neo_Destour" title="Neo Destour">Neo Destour</a>" (New Constitution) party was founded by <a href="/wiki/Habib_Bourguiba" title="Habib Bourguiba">Habib Bourguiba</a> pushing for independence in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. Tunisia became independent in 1955. Its <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Beys_of_Tunis" title="List of Beys of Tunis">bey</a></i> was deposed and <a href="/wiki/Habib_Bourguiba" title="Habib Bourguiba">Habib Bourguiba</a> elected as <a href="/wiki/President_of_Tunisia" title="President of Tunisia">President of Tunisia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELye200297,_264_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELye200297,_264-339">[339]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1954, <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a> deposed the monarchy of Egypt in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_Revolution_of_1952" title="Egyptian Revolution of 1952">Egyptian Revolution of 1952</a> and came to power as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Egypt" title="List of Prime Ministers of Egypt">Prime Minister of Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[340]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Muammar Gaddafi">Muammar Gaddafi</a> led the <a href="/wiki/1969_Libyan_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1969 Libyan coup d'état">1969 Libyan coup d'état</a> which deposed <a href="/wiki/Idris_of_Libya" title="Idris of Libya">Idris of Libya</a>. Gaddafi remained in power until his death in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Libyan_Civil_War_(2011)" title="Libyan Civil War (2011)">Libyan Civil War of 2011</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-341"><a href="#cite_note-341">[341]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt was involved in several wars against Israel and was allied with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_states" title="Arab states">Arab countries</a>. The first was the <a href="/wiki/1948_Arab%E2%80%93Israeli_War" title="1948 Arab–Israeli War">1948 Arab–Israeli War</a>, right after the state of Israel was founded. Egypt went to war again in the <a href="/wiki/Six-Day_War" title="Six-Day War">Six-Day War</a> of 1967 and lost the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> to Israel. They went to war yet again in the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a> of 1973. In 1979, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Egypt" title="List of Presidents of Egypt">President of Egypt</a> <a href="/wiki/Anwar_Sadat" title="Anwar Sadat">Anwar Sadat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_Israel" title="List of Prime Ministers of Israel">Prime Minister of Israel</a> <a href="/wiki/Menachem_Begin" title="Menachem Begin">Menachem Begin</a> signed the <a href="/wiki/Camp_David_Accords" title="Camp David Accords">Camp David Accords</a>, which gave back the Sinai Peninsula to Egypt in exchange for the recognition of Israel. The accords are still in effect today. In 1981, <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Anwar_Sadat" title="Assassination of Anwar Sadat">Sadat was assassinated</a> by members of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Islamic_Jihad" title="Egyptian Islamic Jihad">Egyptian Islamic Jihad</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khalid_Islambouli" title="Khalid Islambouli">Khalid Islambouli</a>. The assassins were <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamists</a> who targeted Sadat for his signing of the Accords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[342]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="926" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png/220px-South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png/330px-South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png/440px-South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:South_Africa_Border_War_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>South African-occupied <a href="/wiki/South_West_Africa" title="South West Africa">South West Africa</a> (1915–1990) and maximum extent of <a href="/wiki/South_African_Border_War" title="South African Border War">South African</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNITA" title="UNITA">UNITA</a> operations in Angola and Zambia during the <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Civil_War" title="Angolan Civil War">Angolan Civil War</a></div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Africa_3">Southern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=81" title="Edit section: Southern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa#History" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa § History</a></div>
<p>In 1948 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa_in_the_Apartheid_Era" title="History of South Africa in the Apartheid Era">apartheid</a> laws were started in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> by the dominant <a href="/wiki/National_Party_(South_Africa)" title="National Party (South Africa)">National Party</a>. These were largely a continuation of existing policies; the difference was the policy of "<a href="/wiki/Apartheid" title="Apartheid">separate development</a>" (Apartheid). Where previous policies had only been disparate efforts to economically exploit the African majority, Apartheid represented an entire philosophy of separate racial goals, leading to both the divisive laws of 'petty apartheid,' and the grander scheme of African homelands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[343]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1994, Apartheid ended, and <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> of the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> was elected president after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_African_general_election,_1994" title="South African general election, 1994">South African general election, 1994</a>, the country's first non-racial election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[344]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Africa_2">Central Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=82" title="Edit section: Central Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="360" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Federal_Republic_of_Southern_Cameroons.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambazonian" title="Ambazonian">Ambazonian</a> nationalism gained steam in 1982</div></div></div><p>The central regions of Africa were traditionally regarded to be the regions between Kilwa and the mouth of the Zambesi river. Due to its isolated position from the coasts, this area has received minimal attention from historian pertaining to Africa. It also had one of the most varied sources of European colonial imperialists including Germany in Cameroon, Britain in Northern Cameroons, Belgium in Congo, and France in CAF. Due to its territory, among the main trope s regarding Central Africa is traversing its lands and the nature of its tropicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[345]</a></sup> Since 1982, one of the main protracted issues within central Africa has been the ongoing secession movement of the secessionist entity of <a href="/wiki/Ambazonia" title="Ambazonia">Ambazonia</a>. The impasse between Cameroon and Ambazonia gained steam in 1992 when Fon Gorji-Dinka filed an international lawsuit against Cameroon claiming that Ambazonian territories are held illegally by the latter and describing Cameroonian claims on Ambazonian territories as illegal. Fifteen years later, this stalemate would escalate when Abmazonia formally declared itself as the Federal Republic of Ambazonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[346]</a></sup>
</p><h4><span class="mw-headline" id="West_Africa_3">West Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=83" title="Edit section: West Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_West_Africa" title="History of West Africa">History of West Africa</a></div>
<p>Following World War II, nationalist movements arose across West Africa, most notably in <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> under <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-347"><a href="#cite_note-347">[347]</a></sup> In 1957, Ghana became the first sub-Saharan colony to achieve its independence, followed the next year by France's colonies; by 1974, West Africa's nations were entirely autonomous. Since independence, many West African nations have been plagued by <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> and instability, with notable <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil wars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>, and a succession of <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">military coups</a> in Ghana and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>. Many states have failed to develop their economies despite enviable natural resources, and political instability is often accompanied by undemocratic government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-349"><a href="#cite_note-349">[349]</a></sup>
</p><p>See also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_Sierra_Leone" title="2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Sierra Leone">2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Sierra Leone</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_Guinea" title="2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Guinea">2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Guinea</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2014_Ebola_virus_epidemic_in_Liberia" title="2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Liberia">2014 Ebola virus epidemic in Liberia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[350]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography_of_British_Africa">Historiography of British Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=84" title="Edit section: Historiography of British Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first historical studies in English appeared in the 1890s, and followed one of four approaches. 1) The territorial narrative was typically written by a veteran soldier or civil servant who gave heavy emphasis to what he had seen. 2) The "apologia" were essays designed to justify British policies. 3) Popularizers tried to reach a large audience. 4) Compendia appeared designed to combine academic and official credentials. Professional scholarship appeared around 1900, and began with the study of business operations, typically using government documents and unpublished archives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-351"><a href="#cite_note-351">[351]</a></sup>
</p><p>The economic approach was widely practiced in the 1930s, primarily to provide descriptions of the changes underway in the previous half-century. In 1935, American historian <a href="/wiki/William_L._Langer" title="William L. Langer">William L. Langer</a> published <i>The Diplomacy of Imperialism: 1890–1902</i>, a book that is still widely cited. In 1939, Oxford professor <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Coupland" title="Reginald Coupland">Reginald Coupland</a> published <i>The Exploitation of East Africa, 1856–1890: The Slave Trade and the Scramble</i>, another popular treatment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>World War II diverted most scholars to wartime projects and accounted for a pause in scholarship during the 1940s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_British_Empire_in_Tropical_Africa_352-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_British_Empire_in_Tropical_Africa-352">[352]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 1950s many African students were studying in British universities, and they produced a demand for new scholarship, and started themselves to supply it as well. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> became the main center for African studies, with activity as well at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a>. The perspective of British government policymakers or international business operations slowly gave way to a new interest in the activities of the natives, especially nationalistic movements and the growing demand for independence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_British_Empire_in_Tropical_Africa_352-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_British_Empire_in_Tropical_Africa-352">[352]</a></sup> The major breakthrough came from <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Robinson" title="Ronald Robinson">Ronald Robinson</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Andrew_Gallagher" title="John Andrew Gallagher">John Andrew Gallagher</a>, especially with their studies of the impact of free trade on Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[353]</a></sup> In 1985 <i>The Oxford History of South Africa</i> (2 vols.) was published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[354]</a></sup> attempting to synthesize the available materials. In 2013, <i>The Oxford Handbook of Modern African History</i> was published,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-355"><a href="#cite_note-355">[355]</a></sup> bringing the scholarship up to date.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_history_of_Africa">Economic history of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=85" title="Edit section: Economic history of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_Africa" title="Economic history of Africa">Economic history of Africa</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_history_of_Africa">Military history of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=86" title="Edit section: Military history of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Africa" title="Military history of Africa">Military history of Africa</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_history_of_Africa">Genetic history of Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=87" title="Edit section: Genetic history of Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_Africa" title="Genetic history of Africa">Genetic history of Africa</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiographic_and_Conceptual_Problems_of_North_Africa_and_Sub-Saharan_Africa">Historiographic and Conceptual Problems of North Africa and Sub-Saharan Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=88" title="Edit section: Historiographic and Conceptual Problems of North Africa and Sub-Saharan Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiographic_and_Conceptual_Problems">Historiographic and Conceptual Problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=89" title="Edit section: Historiographic and Conceptual Problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The current major problem in <a href="/wiki/African_studies" title="African studies">African studies</a> that Mohamed (2010/2012)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> identified is the inherited religious, <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonial</a> paradigm that European Africanists have preserved in present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secularist" title="Secularist">secularist</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-colonial" title="Post-colonial">post-colonial</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglophone" title="Anglophone">Anglophone</a> African <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">historiography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> African and African-American scholars also bear some responsibility in perpetuating this European Africanist preserved <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following conceptualizations of Africa developed by <a href="/wiki/Leo_Africanus" title="Leo Africanus">Leo Africanus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a>, European Africanists conceptually separated continental Africa into two <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)#Colonialism" title="Race (human categorization)">racialized</a> regions – <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> and North Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Sub-Saharan Africa, as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racist" title="Racist">racist</a> geographic construction, serves as an <a href="/wiki/Objectification" title="Objectification">objectified</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compartmentalization_(psychology)" title="Compartmentalization (psychology)">compartmentalized</a> region of "Africa proper", "Africa noire," or "Black Africa."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a> is also considered to be a part of the same racialized construction as Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> North Africa serves as a racialized region of "European Africa", which is conceptually disconnected from Sub-Saharan Africa, and conceptually connected to the Middle East, Asia, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>As a result of these racialized constructions and the conceptual separation of Africa, darker skinned North Africans, such as the so-called <a href="/wiki/Haratin" title="Haratin">Haratin</a>, who have long resided in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, and do not reside south of <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Saharan</a> Africa, have become analogically alienated from their <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Africa" title="Indigenous peoples of Africa">indigeneity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historic" title="Historic">historic</a> reality in North Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> While the origin of the term "Haratin" remains speculative, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exonym" title="Exonym">term</a> may not date much earlier than the 18th century CE and has been involuntarily assigned to darker skinned Maghrebians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Prior to the modern use of the term Haratin as an identifier, and utilized in contrast to bidan or bayd (white), sumr/asmar, suud/<a href="/wiki/Aswad_(name)" title="Aswad (name)">aswad</a>, or Sudan/sudani (black/brown) were <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> terms utilized as identifiers for darker skinned Maghrebians before the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_period" title="Modern period">modern period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> "Haratin" is considered to be an offensive term by the darker skinned Maghrebians it is intended to identify; for example, people in the southern region (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wad_Noun" title="Wad Noun">Wad Noun</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Draa" title="Draa">Draa</a>) of <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> consider it to be an offensive term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-10"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Despite its <a href="/wiki/Historicity" title="Historicity">historicity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Etymology" title="Etymology">etymology</a> being questionable, European colonialists and European Africanists have used the term Haratin as identifiers for groups of "<a href="/wiki/Black_people#Northern_Africa" title="Black people">black</a>" and apparently "<a href="/wiki/Multiracial_people#North_Africa" title="Multiracial people">mixed</a>" people found in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, and Morocco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-11"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Saadian_invasion_of_the_Songhai_Empire" title="Saadian invasion of the Songhai Empire">Saadian invasion of the Songhai Empire</a> serves as the precursor to later narratives that grouped darker skinned Maghrebians together and identified their origins as being Sub-Saharan West Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> serving as a motivation behind the <a href="/wiki/Saadi_Sultanate" title="Saadi Sultanate">Saadian</a> invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a>, this made way for changes in latter behaviors toward dark-skinned Africans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> As a result of changing behaviors toward dark-skinned Africans, darker skinned Maghrebians were forcibly recruited into the army of <a href="/wiki/Ismail_Ibn_Sharif" title="Ismail Ibn Sharif">Ismail Ibn Sharif</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Guard" title="Black Guard">Black Guard</a>, based on the claim of them having descended from enslaved peoples from the times of the Saadian invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> Shurafa historians of the modern period would later utilize these events in narratives about the <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">manumission</a> of enslaved "Hartani" (a vague term, which, by merit of it needing further definition, is implicit evidence for its historicity being questionable).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The narratives derived from Shurafa historians would later become analogically incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Americanization" title="Americanization">Americanized</a> narratives (e.g., the trans-Saharan slave trade, imported enslaved Sub-Saharan West Africans, darker skinned Magrebian freedmen) of the present-day European Africanist paradigm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup>
</p><p>As opposed to having been developed through field research, the <a href="/wiki/Analogy" title="Analogy">analogy</a> in the present-day European Africanist paradigm, which conceptually alienates, dehistoricizes, and <a href="/wiki/Denaturalization" title="Denaturalization">denaturalizes</a> darker skinned North Africans in North Africa and darker skinned Africans throughout the Islamic world at-large, is primarily rooted in an Americanized textual tradition inherited from 19th century European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolitionists" title="Abolitionists">abolitionists</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-12"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Consequently, reliable history, as opposed to an antiquated analogy-based history, for darker skinned North Africans and darker skinned Africans in the Islamic world are limited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-13"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Part of the textual tradition generally associates an inherited status of servant with <a href="/wiki/Dark_skin" title="Dark skin">dark skin</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Negro" title="Negro">Negro</a> labor, Negro cultivators, <a href="/wiki/Negroid" title="Negroid">Negroid</a> slaves, <a href="/wiki/Freedman#Arabian_and_North_African_slavery" title="Freedman">freedman</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-14"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> The European Africanist paradigm uses this as the primary reference point for its construction of origins narratives for darker skinned North Africans (e.g., imported <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Africa" title="Slavery in Africa">slaves</a> from Sub-Saharan <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-15"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> With darker skinned North Africans or darker skinned Africans in the Islamic world treated as an <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">allegory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alterity" title="Alterity">alterity</a>, another part of the textual tradition is the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_slave_trade" title="Trans-Saharan slave trade">trans-Saharan slave trade</a> and their presence in these regions are treated as that of an African <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> in North Africa and the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-16"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Altogether, darker skinned North Africans (e.g., "black" and apparently "mixed" Maghrebians), darker skinned Africans in the Islamic world, the inherited status of servant associated with dark skin, and the trans-Saharan slave trade are conflated and modeled in analogy with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-Americans" title="African-Americans">African-Americans</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Trans-Atlantic slave trade">trans-Atlantic slave trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-17"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>The trans-Saharan slave trade has been used as a <a href="/wiki/Trope_(literature)" title="Trope (literature)">literary device</a> in narratives that analogically explain the origins of darker skinned North Africans in North Africa and the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-18"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Caravans have been equated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_ships" title="Slave ships">slave ships</a>, and the amount of forcibly enslaved Africans transported across the Sahara are alleged to be numerically comparable to the considerably large amount of forcibly enslaved Africans transported across the Atlantic Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-19"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> The simulated narrative of comparable numbers is contradicted by the limited presence of darker skinned North Africans in the present-day Maghreb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-20"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> As part of this simulated narrative, post-classical <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> has also been characterized as having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantations" title="Plantations">plantations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-21"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Another part of this simulated narrative is an Orientalist construction of hypersexualized <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubines" title="Concubines">concubines</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eunuchs" title="Eunuchs">eunuchs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-22"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Concubines in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harems" title="Harems">harems</a> have been used as an explanatory bridge between the allegation of comparable numbers of forcibly enslaved Africans and the limited amount of present-day darker skinned Maghrebians who have been characterized as their diasporic descendants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-23"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> Eunuchs were characterized as sentinels who guarded these harems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The simulated narrative is also based on the major assumption that the indigenous peoples of the Maghreb were once purely <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">white</a> <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a>, who then became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biracial" title="Biracial">biracialized</a> through <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a> with black concubines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-24"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> (existing within a geographic racial binary of pale-skinned Moors residing further north, closer to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean" title="Mediterranean">Mediterranean</a> region, and dark-skinned Moors residing further south, closer to the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The religious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemical" title="Polemical">polemical</a> narrative involving the suffering of enslaved European Christians of the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_slave_trade" title="Barbary slave trade">Barbary slave trade</a> has also been adapted to fit the simulated narrative of a comparable number of enslaved Africans being transported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">slaver</a> caravans, from the south of Saharan Africa, into North Africa and the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-25"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite being an inherited part of the 19th century religious polemical narratives, the use of race in the secularist narrative of the present-day European Africanist paradigm has given the paradigm an appearance of possessing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific" title="Scientific">scientific</a> quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The religious polemical narrative (e.g., holy cause, hostile <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neologisms" title="Neologisms">neologisms</a>) of 19th century European abolitionists about Africa and Africans are silenced, but still preserved, in the secularist narratives of the present-day European Africanist paradigm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_356-26"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed-356">[356]</a></sup> The Orientalist stereotyped hypersexuality of the Moors were viewed by 19th century European abolitionists as deriving from the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The reference to times prior, often used in concert with biblical references, by 19th century European abolitionists, may indicate that realities described of Moors may have been literary fabrications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-10"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The purpose of these apparent literary fabrications may have been to affirm their view of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> as being greater than the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and to affirm the viewpoints held by the readers of their composed works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-11"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The adoption of 19th century European abolitionists' religious polemical narrative into the present-day European Africanist paradigm may have been due to its correspondence with the established textual tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-12"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> The use of stereotyped hypersexuality for Moors are what 19th century European abolitionists and the present-day European Africanist paradigm have in common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-13"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to a lack of considerable development in field research regarding enslavement in Islamic societies, this has resulted in the present-day European Africanist paradigm relying on unreliable estimates for the trans-Saharan slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-14"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> However, insufficient data has also used as a justification for continued use of the faulty present-day European Africanist paradigm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-15"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> Darker skinned Maghrebians, particularly in Morocco, have grown weary of the lack of discretion foreign academics have shown toward them, bear resentment toward the way they have been depicted by foreign academics, and consequently, find the intended activities of foreign academics to be predictable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-16"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup> Rather than continuing to rely on the faulty present-day European Africanist paradigm, Mohamed (2012) recommends revising and improving the current Africanist paradigm (e.g., critical inspection of the origins and introduction of the present characterization of the Saharan caravan; reconsideration of what makes the trans-Saharan slave trade, within its own context in Africa, distinct from the trans-Atlantic slave trade; realistic consideration of the experiences of darker-skinned Maghrebians within their own regional context).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mohamed_II_357-17"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_II-357">[357]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conceptual_Problems">Conceptual Problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=90" title="Edit section: Conceptual Problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Merolla (2017)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> has indicated that the academic study of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> by Europeans developed with North Africa being conceptually subsumed within the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>, whereas, the study of Sub-Saharan Africa was viewed as conceptually distinct from North Africa, and as its own region, viewed as inherently the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> The common pattern of conceptual separation of continental Africa into two regions and the view of conceptual sameness within the region of Sub-Saharan Africa has continued until present-day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-2"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> Yet, with increasing exposure of this problem, discussion about the conceptual separation of Africa has begun to develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-3"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> has served as a trans-regional zone for peoples in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-4"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> Authors from various countries (e.g., Algeria, Cameroon, Sudan) in Africa have critiqued the conceptualization of the Sahara as a regional barrier, and provided counter-arguments supporting the interconnectedness of continental Africa; there are historic and cultural connections as well as trade between <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> (e.g., North Africa with Niger and Mali, North Africa with Tanzania and Sudan, major hubs of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> learning in Niger and Mali).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-5"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> Africa has been conceptually compartmentalized into meaning "Black Africa", "Africa South of the Sahara", and "Sub-Saharan Africa."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-6"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> North Africa has been conceptually "<a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalized</a>" and separated from Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-7"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> While its historic development has occurred within a longer time frame, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemic" title="Epistemic">epistemic</a> development (e.g., form, content) of the present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racialized" title="Racialized">racialized</a> conceptual separation of Africa came as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-8"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup>
</p><p>In African and Berber literary studies, scholarship has remained largely separate from one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-9"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> The conceptual separation of Africa in these studies may be due to how <a href="/wiki/Editing" title="Editing">editing</a> policies of studies in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglophone" title="Anglophone">Anglophone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francophone" title="Francophone">Francophone</a> world are affected by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_politics" title="International politics">international politics</a> of the Anglophone and Francophone world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-10"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> While studies in the Anglophone world have more clearly followed the trend of the conceptual separation of Africa, the Francophone world has been more nuanced, which may stem from imperial policies relating to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_colonialism" title="French colonialism">French colonialism</a> in North Africa and Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-11"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> As the study of North Africa has largely been initiated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabophone" title="Arabophone">Arabophone</a> and Francophone world, denial of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> having become Africanized throughout the centuries it has been present in Africa has shown that the conceptual separation of Africa remains pervasive in the Francophone world; this denial may stem from historic development of the characterization of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> existing as a diametric binary to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Europe" title="Christian Europe">Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-12"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> Among studies in the Francophone world, ties between North Africa and Sub-Saharan Africa have been denied or downplayed, while the ties (e.g., religious, cultural) between the regions and peoples (e.g., Arab language and literature with Berber language and literature) of the Middle East and North Africa have been established by diminishing the differences between the two and selectively focusing on the similarities between the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-13"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> In the Francophone world, construction of racialized regions, such as <a href="/wiki/Black_people#Africa" title="Black people">Black Africa</a> (Sub-Saharan Africans) and <a href="/wiki/White_people" title="White people">White</a> Africa (North Africans, e.g., Berbers and Arabs), has also developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-14"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite having invoked and utilized identities in reference to the racialized conceptualizations of Africa (e.g., North Africa, Sub-Saharan Africa) to oppose imposed identities, <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> have invoked North African identity to oppose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabized" title="Arabized">Arabized</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamization" title="Islamization">Islamicized</a> identities, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africans" title="Sub-Saharan Africans">Sub-Saharan Africans</a> (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negritude" title="Negritude">Negritude</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_Consciousness" title="Black Consciousness">Black Consciousness</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora" title="African diaspora">African diaspora</a> (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_is_Beautiful" title="Black is Beautiful">Black is Beautiful</a>) have invoked and utilized <a href="/wiki/Black_pride" title="Black pride">black</a> identity to oppose <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-15"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup> While Berber studies has largely sought to be establish ties between Berbers and North Africa with Arabs and the Middle East, Merolla (2017) indicated that efforts to establish ties between Berbers and North Africa with Sub-Saharan Africans and Sub-Saharan Africa have recently started to being undertaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merolla_358-16"><a href="#cite_note-Merolla-358">[358]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=91" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=History_of_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=92" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1067248974">.mw-parser-output
